Preservation of the Pledge and the Boundary Between Keeping and Using
Rulings on the pledgee's duty of preservation and the boundary between preservation and use.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between preservation (hifdh) and use (isti'mal) of a pledged collateral and determine when a pledgee becomes liable for loss based on customary practice.

Core Idea
The pledgee is liable only when the collateral is held in a customary place of use, not preservation.

A pledgee is entrusted with the collateral as a trust. He must preserve it, but what counts as preservation vs. use? The Hanafi school draws a fine line based on custom. This lesson unpacks the boundary, using examples from rings and garments to show when a pledgee incurs liability.

Guided Path
1
State the basic duty of the pledgee
The pledgee must preserve the collateral as a trust. He may delegate preservation to his household (wife, children, servants). He is not liable for customary preservation.
The collateral is a trust (amanah), like a deposit (wadi'ah).
2
Understand the boundary principle
The principle from al-Muntaqa: holding for preservation = no liability; holding for use = liability. The boundary is whether the object is held in a place customarily used for that purpose.
Custom ('urf) determines the classification.
3
Analyze the example of women's ornaments
Wearing anklets in the usual place (feet) is use; wearing them as bracelets is preservation. Similarly, wrapping a shirt as turban is preservation; putting on a bracelet in the usual place is use.
The physical position matters: if the item is placed where it is not typically used, it is preservation.
4
Analyze the example of rings
Wearing a ring on the right little finger (customary for adornment) is use. Wearing it on other fingers or for sealing (e.g., above another ring) is preservation. Local custom may override (e.g., wearing a second ring for adornment in some regions).
The intention and local practice affect the ruling.
5
Apply to agent/executor
An agent or executor who receives a pledge is a trustee, not the contracting party. He is not liable for loss if he did not use it, because he holds it only as a trust for the creditor.
This follows from the principle that the pledgee's liability arises from use, not from mere holding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : ويحفظ بنفسه وزوجته وولده وخادمه الذي في عياله معناه أن يكون الولد أيضاً في عياله لأن عينه أمانة على ما بينا فصار كالوديعة وأجيره الخاص كولده الذي في عياله وهو الذي استأجره مشاهرة أو مساهنة والمعبر فيه المساكنة ولا عبرة بالنفقة حتى إن المرأة لو دفعته إلى زوجها لا تضمن. قال في المنتقى : الأصل أن المرتهن أو المستأجر متى أمسك العين للحفظ لا يضمن ومتى أمسكها للاستعمال يضمن، فالحد الفاصل بينهما . أنه هو متی أمسك الشيء في موضع لا يمسك فيه إلا للاستعمال والانتفاع في ذلك الموضع فهو استعمال، وإذا أمسكه في موضع لا يمسكه فيه للاستعمال فهو حفظ، فعلى هذا قالوا : إذا تسورت بالخلخال أو تخلخلت بالسوار أو تعمم بالقميص أو وضع العمامة على العاتق فهذا كله حفظ وليس باستعمال لأن الاستعمال للإمساك في موضع لا يمسك للاستعمال فكان الإمساك للحفظ وإذا تسور بالسوار وما أشبهه ضمن لأن الإمساك وجد في موضع للاستعمال فكان استعمالاً وحفظاً. وروي عن محمد رحمه الله تعالى : الرهن إذا كان خاتماً فتختم به في الخنصر اليمني يضمن لأن من يتختم في يمينه للزينة، وإن تختم فوق خاتم في ذلك الأصبح لا يضمن. قيل لمحمد : الناس يستعملون خاتمين في خنصر واحد قال : إنما يستعملونه للختم لا للزينة. قال مشايخنا : وهذا في بلادهم، وأما في بلادنا فقد يستعملون الثاني للزينة : قال مشايخنا: يجب أن يضمن، وإن تختم في أصبح غير الخنصر لا يضمن لأنه لا يستعمل كذلك قط استعمال الزينة . قال بعض مشايخنا : إذا تختم وجعل الفص مما يلي الكف لم يضمن وكان حفظاً لا استعمالاً. الوكيل يقبض الدين إذا أخذ الرهن ممن عليه الدين فضاع عنده أو الوصي إذا أخذ رهناً من غريم للميت بدين عليه والورثة كبار فضاع عنده قال محمد رحمه الله تعالى : لا ضمان عليه لأنه لم يل الإقراض والأداء وإنما قبضه على أن يكون أميناً فيه لصاحب الدين .
He (the author) said: He preserves it by himself, his wife, his child, and his servant who is in his care. Meaning that the child should also be in his care because the object is a trust, as we explained, so it becomes like a deposit. His specific employee is like his child in his care; he is the one he hired monthly or yearly, and the criterion is cohabitation, not expenditure. So if a woman gives it to her husband, she is not liable. He said in al-Muntaqa: The principle is that the pledgee or lessee, whenever he holds the object for preservation, he is not liable; and whenever he holds it for use, he is liable. The boundary between them is: whenever he holds the thing in a place where it is not held except for use and benefit, it is use; and if he holds it in a place where he does not hold it for use, it is preservation. According to this, they said: If she puts on anklets (as bracelets) or wears bangles on her legs, or wraps a shirt as a turban, or places a turban on the shoulder, all this is preservation, not use, because use is holding in a place not meant for use, so the holding is for preservation. If she puts on a bracelet (in the usual place), she becomes liable because the holding is in a place of use, so it is both use and preservation. It is narrated from Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him): If the pledge is a ring and he wears it on the right little finger, he is liable because whoever wears it on the right hand does so for adornment. If he wears it over another ring on the same finger, he is not liable. It was said to Muhammad: People wear two rings on one little finger. He said: They only wear them for sealing, not for adornment. Our masters said: This is in their country; in our country, they may wear the second for adornment. Our masters said: He must be liable. If he wears it on a finger other than the little finger, he is not liable because it is never used for adornment in that way. Some of our masters said: If he wears the ring with the seal facing the palm, he is not liable; it is preservation, not use. The agent collecting the debt, if he takes a pledge from the debtor and it is lost with him, or the executor if he takes a pledge from the creditor of the deceased for a debt due, and the heirs are adults, and it is lost with him, Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him) said: There is no liability on him because he was not involved in lending and repayment; he only took it to be a trustee for the debt-owner.
Source pages p. 440

Commentary
This passage details the pledgee's duty of preservation and the critical distinction between preservation and use, which determines liability. The pledgee may preserve the collateral through himself, his wife, children, and household servants. A hired servant is treated like a child if living in the same household. The key principle from al-Muntaqa: holding an object in a customary place of preservation (e.g., wearing anklets on legs, wrapping a shirt as a turban) is preservation and not liable; holding it in a customary place of use (e.g., wearing a ring on the finger for adornment) constitutes use and incurs liability. The text provides detailed examples from Muhammad regarding rings and local customs. Finally, it states that an agent or executor who receives a pledge as a trustee is not liable for loss, as they are not the ones who contracted the debt.

Key Terms
حفظ (hifdh): Preservation; holding the collateral in a manner that is customary for safekeeping, not for personal benefit.
استعمال (isti'mal): Use; placing the collateral in a position meant for benefit, typically leading to liability if done without permission.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A pledgee wears a pledged ring on his left little finger (not customary for adornment in his region). The ring is lost. Is he liable?
A) Yes, because any wearing is use.
B) No, because it is not customary adornment.
C) Yes, because he should have kept it in a box.
D) No, because he is always a trustee.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school uses local custom to draw the line between preservation (no liability) and use (liability) for a pledgee.

Liability of Pledgee When Depositing Collateral with Another
Ruling with qiyas proof on pledgee's liability for depositing collateral elsewhere

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the liability of a pledgee who entrusts the collateral to a third party and apply the Hanafi rule regarding substitution of the pledge.

Core Idea
A pledgee who deposits the collateral with another is liable for its full value, and the assessed value becomes a substitute pledge.

This lesson examines the liability of a pledgee who, without necessity, deposits the pledged item with a third party. The Hanafi school treats the collateral as a trust (amanah) in the pledgee's hands; any unauthorized transfer is a transgression (ta'addi) and makes the pledgee liable for the entire value. The text also explains the legal consequences when a judge assesses the value—whether it becomes a new pledge or is set off against the debt.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act: the pledgee deposits the collateral with another person. The ruling: he is liable for the full value of the collateral.
Liability arises due to transgression (ta'addi).
2
Understand the underlying principle
The collateral in the pledgee's hands is a trust (wadi'ah). Any unauthorized use or transfer is a transgression, making the pledgee a guarantor (damin) of the value.
This is derived from the general rule that a trustee becomes liable if they transgress.
3
Apply qiyas (analogy) to the case of deposit
The pledgee's act is compared to a depositee who deposits the trust with another without permission. In both cases, the original holder is liable for the full value as if it were usurped.
The effective cause (illa) is the unauthorized transfer of a trust.
4
Examine the consequences of a court judgment
If the judge assesses the value and it is of the same type as the debt, set-off occurs if the debt is due; if deferred, the value becomes a new pledge. If the value is of a different type, it becomes a pledge until the debt is paid.
The substitute (badal) takes the ruling of the original collateral.
5
Note the unresolved liability to the second depositee
The text refers to the Book of Deposits for the discussion on whether the pledgee is also liable to the person he deposited with, indicating a difference of opinion.
This is not elaborated here due to the cut-off.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (وضمن بحفظه بغيره وبإيداعه وتعديه قيمته لما بينا أن عينه وديعة والوديعة تضمن بهذه الأشياء لكونه متعدياً بها فيضمن جميع قيمته كالمغصوب. وهل يضمن للمودع الثاني؟ فهو على الخلاف الذي بيناه في مودع المودع في كتاب الوديعة. ثم إن قضى القاضي بالقيمة من جنس الدين يلتقيان قصاصاً بمجرد القضاء إذا كان الدين حالاً فلا يطالب كل واحد منهما صاحبه إلا بالفضل، وإن كان مؤجلاً يضمن المرتهن قيمته ويكون رهناً عنده لأنه بدل الرهن فيكون له حكم أصله، فإذا حل الأجل أخذه بدينه، وإن قضى بالقيمة من خلاف جنس الدين كان رهناً عنده إلى أن يقضيه دينه لأنه بدل الرهن فأخذ حكمه. ولو رهن خاتماً عند امرأة فجعلت خاتماً فوق خاتم تضمن لأن النساء يلبسن كذلك فيكون من باب
He (the author) said: And he is liable for preservation by someone else, by depositing, and by transgressing its value, because we made clear that the object is a deposit, and a deposit is liable by these things due to transgression, so he guarantees its entire value like a usurped object. And is he liable to the second depositee? It follows the difference we explained about the depositor of the depositor in the book of deposits. Then if the judge assesses the value from the same type as the debt, they cancel each other by set-off immediately upon the judgment if the debt is due; then neither party claims from the other except the surplus. If the debt is deferred, the pledgee guarantees its value, and it becomes a pledge with him because it is the substitute of the pledge, so it takes the ruling of its origin. When the term ends, he takes it for his debt. If the judge assesses the value from a different type than the debt, it remains a pledge with him until he pays his debt because it is the substitute of the pledge, so it takes its ruling. And if he pledges a ring to a woman and she places a ring over [another] ring, she is liable because women wear them like that, so it is from the category...
Source pages p. 440

Commentary
This passage discusses the pledgee's liability when he entrusts the collateral to a third party for preservation or deposits it elsewhere, which is considered a transgression (ta'addi) because the object is a trust (wadi'ah) in his hands. The ruling is compared to a deposit: the pledgee becomes liable for the full value as if he had usurped it. The text then addresses the question of whether he is also liable to the second depositee, which is a matter of difference similar to the discussion in the Book of Deposits. It then explains the consequences of a court judgment: if the value assessed is of the same type as the debt, the two are set off if the debt is due; if the debt is deferred, the value becomes a new pledge. If the value is of a different type, it remains a pledge until the debt is paid. The text ends mid-sentence about a ring placed over another ring by a woman, implying she becomes liable because that is customary use for women.

Key Terms
وديعة (wadi'ah): A deposit; an object held in trust for safekeeping.
بدل الرهن (badal al-rahn): The substitute for the pledge; e.g., the value assessed by the court when the pledge is lost due to transgression.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A pledgee deposits the collateral with a third party without permission. The judge assesses the value, which is of the same genus as the debt. The debt is deferred. What happens?
A) The value and debt are set off immediately.
B) The value becomes a new pledge until the term ends.
C) The pledgee must pay the value now.
D) The third party becomes liable.

Key Takeaway
A pledgee who deposits collateral without permission is liable for its full value, and the assessed value substitutes as a new pledge unless the debt is due and of same genus, where set-off occurs.

Preserving vs Using the Pawn: The Hanafi Criterion for Liability
Ruling with proof explaining the preservation-use distinction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi criterion of preservation versus use to determine whether a mortgagee is liable for the pawn.

Core Idea
Preserving the pawn as a trust does not create liability; using it in the place of normal use does.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq establishes a clear principle: the mortgagee (creditor holding a pawn) is a trustee, not a guarantor. Liability only arises if he uses the pawn for his own benefit. The text provides a physical criterion—whether the item is held in a place normally used for that type of item—and applies it to clothing, jewelry, and rings. This lesson unpacks the rule, its examples, and the role of local custom as recognized by later Hanafi scholars.

Guided Path
1
Identify the General Rule and the Trust Status
The mortgagee may keep the pawn himself, with his wife, child, or household servant. These are all trustees (amanah). If he deposits the pawn elsewhere or handles it improperly, he becomes liable. The key is that preservation is free from liability.
The pawn is like a deposit (wadi‘ah) in terms of trust.
2
Understand the Dividing Line: Preservation vs Use
Al-Muntaqa states: if the mortgagee holds the item for preservation (in a place not intended for its use), no liability; if he holds it for use (in a place intended for its use), he is liable. The place of holding is the objective test.
The criterion is physical, not subjective intention.
3
Apply the Criterion to the Examples
Wearing anklets as bracelets or a turban on the shoulder is preservation (no liability) because these are not the normal places of use. But wearing a bracelet on the wrist or a ring on the right little finger for adornment is use (liability).
The examples illustrate the rule concretely.
4
Analyze the Intra-Hanafi Ikhtilaf on Rings
Imam Muhammad ruled that wearing a ring over another ring on the same finger is not use (unless for adornment by custom). Later Hanafi scholars added that local custom must be considered: if in a land people wear the second ring for adornment, the mortgagee is liable.
This shows how custom ('urf) can modify the ruling.
5
Conclude with the Mufta Bihi and Practical Application
The relied-upon position is that liability depends on whether the act is considered use in that locality. The mortgagee is not liable for simple preservation, but must avoid any handling that would be considered use by custom.
For example, an agent or executor collecting a debt is also a trustee and not liable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الرهن وولده وخادمه الذي في عياله وضمن بحفظه بغيره وبإيداعه وتعديه قيمته وأجرة بيت رد الرهن ولا يبطل الرهن بموت الراهن ولا بموت المرتهن ولا بموتهما وبقي الرهن رهناً عند الورثة وسيأتي له مزيد .بيان قال رحمه الله : ويحفظ بنفسه وزوجته وولده وخادمه الذي في عياله معناه أن يكون الولد أيضاً في عياله لأن عينه أمانة على ما بينا فصار كالوديعة وأجيره الخاص كولده الذي في عياله وهو الذي استأجره مشاهرة أو مساهنة والمعبر فيه المساكنة ولا عبرة بالنفقة حتى إن المرأة لو دفعته إلى زوجها لا تضمن. قال في المنتقى : الأصل أن المرتهن أو المستأجر متى أمسك العين للحفظ لا يضمن ومتى أمسكها للاستعمال يضمن، فالحد الفاصل بينهما . أنه هو متی أمسك الشيء في موضع لا يمسك فيه إلا للاستعمال والانتفاع في ذلك الموضع فهو استعمال، وإذا أمسكه في موضع لا يمسكه فيه للاستعمال فهو حفظ، فعلى هذا قالوا : إذا تسورت بالخلخال أو تخلخلت بالسوار أو تعمم بالقميص أو وضع العمامة على العاتق فهذا كله حفظ وليس باستعمال لأن الاستعمال للإمساك في موضع لا يمسك للاستعمال فكان الإمساك للحفظ وإذا تسور بالسوار وما أشبهه ضمن لأن الإمساك وجد في موضع للاستعمال فكان استعمالاً وحفظاً. وروي عن محمد رحمه الله تعالى : الرهن إذا كان خاتماً فتختم به في الخنصر اليمني يضمن لأن من يتختم في يمينه للزينة، وإن تختم فوق خاتم في ذلك الأصبح لا يضمن. قيل لمحمد : الناس يستعملون خاتمين في خنصر واحد قال : إنما يستعملونه للختم لا للزينة. قال مشايخنا : وهذا في بلادهم، وأما في بلادنا فقد يستعملون الثاني للزينة : قال مشايخنا: يجب أن يضمن، وإن تختم في أصبح غير الخنصر لا يضمن لأنه لا يستعمل كذلك قط استعمال الزينة . قال بعض مشايخنا : إذا تختم وجعل الفص مما يلي الكف لم يضمن وكان حفظاً لا استعمالاً. الوكيل يقبض الدين إذا أخذ الرهن ممن عليه الدين فضاع عنده أو الوصي إذا أخذ رهناً من غريم للميت بدين عليه والورثة كبار فضاع عنده قال محمد رحمه الله تعالى : لا ضمان عليه لأنه لم يل الإقراض والأداء وإنما قبضه على أن يكون أميناً فيه لصاحب الدين .
Book of Pledge: And his child and his servant who is in his household, and he is liable if he keeps it with others, deposits it, or misuses it, its value and the rent of a house. The pledge is not invalidated by the death of the mortgagor, nor by the death of the mortgagee, nor by the death of both, and the pledge remains a pledge with the heirs, and more will come. Explanation: He (the author) said: He keeps it by himself, his wife, his child, and his servant who is in his household. Meaning that the child is also in his household because the item is a trust as we explained, so it becomes like a deposit. His specific hired worker is like his child in his household, i.e., the one he hires by the month or the year, and what is considered is cohabitation, not expenditure. Even if a woman gives it to her husband, she is not liable. Al-Muntaqa said: The principle is that the mortgagee or lessee, whenever he holds the item for preservation, is not liable, and whenever he holds it for use, he is liable. The dividing line between them: whenever he holds something in a place that is not held except for use and benefit in that place, it is use; and if he holds it in a place that is not held for use, it is preservation. Based on this, they said: If she wears anklets as bracelets, or wears a bracelet as anklets, or wraps a shirt as a turban, or places a turban on the shoulder, all this is preservation and not use, because use is for holding in a place that is not held for use; so the holding is for preservation. But if he wears a bracelet as a bracelet and the like, he is liable because the holding occurred in a place for use, so it was use and preservation. It is narrated from Muhammad: If the pledge is a ring and he wears it on the right little finger, he is liable because whoever wears a ring on the right hand does so for adornment. If he wears it over a ring on that same finger, he is not liable. It was said to Muhammad: People wear two rings on one little finger. He said: They only use them for sealing, not adornment. Our scholars said: This is in their land; as for our land, they may use the second for adornment. Our scholars said: He must be liable. And if he wears it on a finger other than the little finger, he is not liable because it is not used as adornment at all. Some of our scholars said: If he wears the ring and puts the bezel towards the palm, he is not liable, and it is preservation not use. An agent who collects a debt: if he takes the pledge from the debtor and it is lost with him, or an executor takes a pledge from the deceased's debtor for a debt and the adult heirs are present, and it is lost with him, Muhammad said: There is no liability on him because he did not perform the lending and payment; he took it to be a trustee for the creditor.
Source pages p. 440

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the Book of Pledge, explaining who may keep the pawn without liability. The author (al-Kanz) states the general ruling: the mortgagee may keep the pawn himself, with his wife, his child, and his servant in his household. The commentary (al-Bahr al-Raiq) clarifies that these individuals are also trustees (amanah), likening the pawn to a deposit (wadi'ah). The hired worker is treated like a child if he lives in the household. The key principle from al-Muntaqa is that liability depends on the purpose of holding: if for preservation, no liability; if for use, liability arises. The dividing line is whether the item is held in a place normally used for that type of use. Examples illustrate: wearing anklets as bracelets is preservation, but wearing a bracelet as a bracelet is use. For rings, wearing on the right little finger is use (adornment), while wearing over another ring on the same finger is not use unless local custom dictates otherwise. If the mortgagee is an agent or executor collecting a debt, he is not liable if the pawn is lost because he is a trustee.

Key Terms
رهن (rahn): A pawn or pledge held as security for a debt.
مرتهن (murtahin): The creditor who holds the pawn as security.
حفظ (hifz): Preservation or custody of the pawn without using it for benefit.
استعمال (isti'mal): Using the pawn for personal benefit beyond mere preservation.
ضمان (daman): Liability to compensate for the value of the pawn if lost or damaged.
أمانة (amanah): A trust; the pawn is held in trust by the mortgagee.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the dividing line between preservation (hifz) and use (isti'mal) for the purpose of mortgagee liability?
A) Whether the mortgagee intends to benefit from the pawn.
B) Whether the pawn is held in a place normally used for that type of item.
C) Whether the mortgagee has the owner's permission to handle the pawn.
D) Whether the pawn is lost or damaged.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school determines mortgagee liability by a physical, objective test: holding the pawn in its normal place of use constitutes use and triggers liability; otherwise, it is preserved trust without liability.

intermediate
8 min
Mortgagee's Liability for Transgression (Ta'addi) on the Pawn
Mortgagee's liability for transgression on pawn

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state when a mortgagee is liable for the pawn, apply the rule of transgression to specific scenarios (ring, cloak, swords, produce), and identify the exception for emergency sale.

Core Idea
The mortgagee is liable for the pawn if he uses or deposits it without permission, but necessity permits sale without liability.

In Hanafi fiqh, the pawn (rahn) is held by the mortgagee as a trust (amanah). Any act beyond preservation—such as using it, depositing it with others, or selling it without authorization—is transgression (ta'addi) that creates liability for the full value. However, Islamic law recognizes necessity: in emergencies, the mortgagee may take protective measures like picking fruit or even selling the pawn without incurring liability. This lesson unpacks the rules through practical examples from the classical texts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and liability ruling
For each action of the mortgagee (depositing with others, using, selling), determine whether it is permitted or constitutes transgression and whether liability follows.
The default liability arises from ta'addi beyond preservation.
2
Understand the legal basis
The pawn is treated as a deposit (wadi'ah). Transgression in a deposit creates liability by analogy to usurpation (ghasb). This is the qiyas that establishes the rule.
The ruling is derived from the pawn's nature as a trust.
3
Apply to ring, cloak, and swords examples
Examine the specific actions: wearing a ring over another ring, wearing a cloak normally, girding two swords vs. three. Liability depends on whether the use is customary (customary use = liability) or merely preservation.
Custom and explicit permission can negate liability.
4
Apply to selling or dealing with produce
Normal sale of pawn or its produce without permission is invalid and creates liability. But in fear of spoilage, picking fruit or milking is allowed by istihsan—it is considered preservation, not use.
Istihsan overrides strict analogy here.
5
Recognize the emergency exception
In extreme necessity (absent owner and judge, fear of loss), the mortgagee is implicitly permitted to sell the pawn; he is not liable. This is a concession to prevent total loss.
This is based on a narration from Imam Muhammad.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (وضمن بحفظه بغيره وبإيداعه وتعديه قيمته لما بينا أن عينه وديعة والوديعة تضمن بهذه الأشياء لكونه متعدياً بها فيضمن جميع قيمته كالمغصوب. وهل يضمن للمودع الثاني؟ فهو على الخلاف الذي بيناه في مودع المودع في كتاب الوديعة. ثم إن قضى القاضي بالقيمة من جنس الدين يلتقيان قصاصاً بمجرد القضاء إذا كان الدين حالاً فلا يطالب كل واحد منهما صاحبه إلا بالفضل، وإن كان مؤجلاً يضمن المرتهن قيمته ويكون رهناً عنده لأنه بدل الرهن فيكون له حكم أصله، فإذا حل الأجل أخذه بدينه، وإن قضى بالقيمة من خلاف جنس الدين كان رهناً عنده إلى أن يقضيه دينه لأنه بدل الرهن فأخذ حكمه. ولو رهن خاتماً عند امرأة فجعلت خاتماً فوق خاتم تضمن لأن النساء يلبسن كذلك فيكون من باب الاستعمال بغير إذن المالك، وكذا الطيلسان إن لبسه لبساً معتاداً ،ضمن، ولو وضعه على عنقه لم يضمن. وفي الواقعات رجل رهن عند رجل خاتماً وقال للمرتهن تختم به إن أمره أن يتختم به في الخنصر فهلك في حال التختم بهلك بالدين لأنه أمر بالحفظ لا بالاستعمال. وفي الذخيرة هو الصحيح. ولو رهنه سيفين فتلقدهما ضمن قال فخر الدين: والفتوى على أنه يضمن، وفي الثلاثة لا يضمن لأن العادة جرت بين الشجعان بتقليد السيفين في الحرب دون الثلاثة. وفي المحيط : ولو باع المرتهن زوائد الرهن بغير إذن الراهن أو القاضي لم يجز بيعه ويضمن قيمته، وإن خاف تلفه فجذ الثمار وحلب اللبن جاز استحساناً لأنه نوع من الحفظ، فإن خاف تلفه عنده فأمسكه يرفع الأمر إلى القاضي حتى يبيعه أو يأذن له في البيع إن كان المالك غائباً، وإن كان حاضراً يرجع إليه، ولو كان المرتهن بعيداً من القاضي والمالك وخاف التلف فباعه بنفسه لم يضمن هكذا روي عن محمد لأنه مأذون له في مثل هذه الحالة في البيع دلالة وليس للمرتهن ولا للراهن أن يزرع الأرض ولا أن يؤجرها لأنه ليس له الانتفاع بالرهن.
He (the author) said: (And he is liable if he keeps it with others, deposits it, or misuses its value) because we have established that the item is a deposit, and a deposit incurs liability through these things due to transgressing, so he is liable for its entire value like a usurped item. And is he liable to the second depositor? That is subject to the difference we explained about the depositor of a deposit in the Book of Deposit. Then if the judge awards its value in the same type as the debt, they offset by mere judgment if the debt is due, so neither claims from the other except the surplus; but if the debt is deferred, the mortgagee is liable for its value and it becomes a pledge with him, because it is a substitute for the pawn and takes its ruling, so when the term comes, he takes it for his debt. If the judge awards value in a different type than the debt, it is a pledge with him until he pays his debt, because it is a substitute for the pawn and takes its ruling. And if a man pawns a ring with a woman, and she puts a ring over a ring on her finger, she is liable because women wear like that, so it falls under use without the owner's permission. Similarly, the taylasan: if he wears it in a customary manner, he is liable; if he places it on his neck, he is not liable. In al-Waqi'at: A man pawned a ring with another and said to the mortgagee: 'Wear it on the little finger.' If he ordered him to wear it on the little finger, and it perished while worn, he is not liable, because he ordered preservation not use. In al-Dhakhira, this is the correct view. And if he pawns two swords and girds them both on, he is liable; Fakhr al-Din said: The fatwa is that he is liable. For three, he is not liable, because custom among brave men is to gird two swords in war, not three. In al-Muhit: If the mortgagee sells the excess of the pawn without the permission of the mortgagor or the judge, his sale is not permissible and he is liable for its value. But if he fears its loss and picks the fruit or milks the milk, it is permissible by istihsan because it is a type of preservation. If he fears its loss in his possession, he should refer the matter to the judge to sell it or permit him to sell, if the owner is absent. If the owner is present, he refers to him. And if the mortgagee is far from the judge and the owner and fears loss, and sells it himself, he is not liable; thus it is narrated from Muhammad, because he is implicitly permitted to sell in such a situation. Neither the mortgagee nor the mortgagor may cultivate the land nor lease it, because they do not have the right to benefit from the pawn.
Source pages p. 440

Commentary
This passage explains the mortgagee's liability for depositing or using the pawn. The author states that if the mortgagee keeps the pawn with someone else, deposits it, or misuses it, he is liable for its full value, similar to a usurper. The text then discusses the scenario where the judge awards compensation: if the compensation matches the debt type, they offset; if deferred, the compensation becomes a new pledge. The example of a woman wearing a ring over another ring shows that wearing customarily is considered use without permission, leading to liability. For a taylasan (cloak), wearing it normally is use, but placing it on the neck is preservation. The incident from al-Waqi'at shows that if the mortgagee is explicitly told to wear the ring on the little finger, and it perishes, he is not liable because the instruction was for preservation. Girding two swords is use (liable), but three swords is not customary, hence no liability. Selling excess produce without permission is invalid, but in emergency, like fear of spoilage, picking fruit or milking is permissible by istihsan. In extreme emergency when the owner and judge are absent, the mortgagee may sell the pawn without liability, based on implicit permission.

Key Terms
تعدي (ta'addi): Transgression or misuse beyond what is allowed as a trustee.
قيمة (qimah): The monetary value of the pawn, to be compensated if liable.
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference overriding strict analogy; here used to permit selling produce in emergency.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, in which scenario is a mortgagee NOT liable for selling the pawn without permission?
A) When he sells the excess produce after picking it.
B) When he is far from the judge and owner and fears the pawn will be lost.
C) When he sells it to pay off the debt early.
D) When he sells it with the intention of using the proceeds for preservation.

Key Takeaway
Transgression (ta'addi) on a pawn creates liability for its full value, but custom and necessity (istihsan) can limit or remove liability.

Allocation of Pawn Expenses: Preservation vs. Benefit
Ruling with principle-based classification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle of pawn expense allocation to classify any given expense as borne by the mortgagor or the mortgagee.

Core Idea
Preservation expenses are on the mortgagee; benefit expenses are on the mortgagor.

In a pawn (rahn) contract, the pawned item generates various expenses. Who bears them? Hanafi fiqh provides a clear principle based on the purpose of the expense. This lesson unpacks that principle through the commentary of al-Bahr al-Raiq.

Guided Path
1
Extract the basic ruling
Read the matn: storage rent and guard on mortgagee; shepherd, upkeep, kharaj on mortgagor.
This gives the concrete examples from which the principle is derived.
2
Discover the principle
Identify the two categories: expenses for the pawn's own benefit (maintenance, feeding, etc.) are on the owner (mortgagor); expenses for preservation and returning the pawn to the creditor are on the mortgagee.
The commentator explicitly states: 'ما يحتاج إليه لمصلحة الرهن لنفسه وتبقيته فهو على الراهن' and 'ما كان لحفظه أو لرده ... فهو على المرتهن'.
3
Apply to listed examples
Categorize given expenses (clothing, wet nurse, irrigation, etc.) under the two principles.
Check each example against the principle to ensure consistent application.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi difference
Abu Yusuf considers housing rent (ujrat al-ma'wa) as on mortgagor like upkeep; the majority holds it on mortgagee as preservation cost.
The mufta bihi is the majority view; Abu Yusuf's view is marjuh (weak) for fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : ( وأجرة بيت الحفظ وحافظه على المرتهن وأجرة راعيه ونفقته والخراج على الراهن والأصل فيه أن ما يحتاج إليه لمصلحة الرهن لنفسه وتبقيته فهو على الراهن، سواء كان في فصل أو لم يكن لأن العين باقية على ملكه وكذا منافعه مملوكة له فيكون أصلاً وتبقيته عليه لما أنه مؤنة ملكه كما في الوديعة وذلك مثل النفقة مأكله من ومشربه وأجرة الراعي مثله لأنه علف البهائم، ومن هذا الجنس كسوة الرقيق وأجرة ظئر ولد الرهن وكري النهر وكسر النهر وسقي البساتين وتلقيح نخيله وجذاذها والقيام بمصالحه. وفي النوازل : أبي الراهن أن ينفق على الرهن فالقاضي يأمر المرتهن بالنفقة، فإذا قبض الدين فللمرتهن أن يحبسه على النفقة فإن هلك في هذه الحالة فالنفقة على الراهن وكل ما كان لحفظه أو لرده إلى يد المرتهن أو لرد جزء منه كمداواة الجرح فهو على المرتهن مثل أجرة الحافظ لأن الإمساك حق له والحفظ واجب عليه فتكون مؤنته عليه، وكذلك أجرة البيت التي يحفظ فيه الرهن. وعن أبي يوسف أن أجرة المأوى على الراهن بمنزلة النفقة، ومن هذا القسم جعل الآبق إذا كان كله مضموناً لأن يد الاستيفاء كانت ثابتة على المحل ويحتاج إلى إعادة يد الاستيفاء ليرده على المالك فكانت من مؤنة الرد فتكون عليه. وإن كان بعضه أمانة فيقدر المضمون على المرتهن وحصة الأمانة على الراهن ولأن الرد لإعادة اليد ويده في الزيادة يد المالك إذ هو كالمودع فيها فتكون على المالك بخلاف أجرة البيت الذي يحفظ فيه الرهن فإن كلها تجب على المرتهن كيفما كان لأن وجوبها لأجل الحبس وحق الحبس ثابت له في الكل. وأما الجعل فلأجل الضمان فيتقدر بقدره، والمداواة والفداء من الجناية ينقسم على المضمون والأمانة والخراج على الراهن لأنه مؤنة الملك والعشر فيما يخرج مقدم على حق المرتهن لتعلقه
He said: (The rent of the storage house and its guard are upon the mortgagee, and the rent of the shepherd, its upkeep, and the land tax are upon the mortgagor.) The principle is that what is needed for the benefit of the pawn itself and its preservation is upon the mortgagor, whether seasonal or not, because the object remains his property, and likewise its benefits are owned by him, so he is the principal and its preservation is upon him as maintenance of his property, like in a deposit. That includes food and drink, the rent of a shepherd because it is feed for animals, and of this kind: clothing for slaves, wet nurse wages for the pawn's child, digging canals, breaking canals, irrigating orchards, pollinating palm trees, picking dates, and managing its interests. In al-Nawazil: If the mortgagor refuses to spend on the pawn, the judge orders the mortgagee to spend. Then when he collects the debt, the mortgagee may detain him for the expenses. If the pawn perishes in that state, the expenses are on the mortgagor. Everything that is for its preservation or returning it to the mortgagee's hand, or returning a part of it like treating a wound, is upon the mortgagee, like the guard's wage, because the right of possession is his and preservation is obligatory upon him, so its maintenance is upon him. Likewise the rent of the house where the pawn is kept. From Abu Yusuf: The rent of housing is upon the mortgagor like upkeep. And of this category: the reward for recovering a runaway slave if it is wholly liable, because the hand of recovery was established on the place and needs to re-establish the hand of recovery to return it to the owner, so it is part of the cost of return, thus upon the mortgagee. If part of it is trust, the liable part is apportioned to the mortgagee and the trust portion to the mortgagor, because the return is to re-establish the hand, and his hand over the increase is the owner's hand, as he is a trustee therein, so it's upon the owner; unlike the rent of the house where the pawn is kept, which is entirely upon the mortgagee regardless, because it is due for the sake of detention and the right of detention is established in all of it. As for the reward, it is for liability and is proportioned accordingly. The wound treatment and ransom from injury are divided between the liable and trust portions. The land tax is upon the mortgagor because it is a maintenance of ownership, and the 'ushr (tithe) on what comes out takes precedence over the mortgagee's right because it is attached.
Source pages p. 441

Commentary
This passage from the Book of Pledge details the allocation of expenses between the mortgagor (owner) and the mortgagee (creditor). The original ruling (matn) states: the rent of the storage house and its guard are on the mortgagee; the rent of the shepherd, the pawn's upkeep, and the land tax (kharaj) are on the mortgagor. The commentary explains the principle: expenses that serve the benefit and preservation of the pawn itself are on the mortgagor because he owns the pawn and its benefits. Examples include food, shepherd's rent, clothing for slaves, wet nurse for the pawn's offspring, agricultural costs. If the mortgagor refuses to spend, the judge may order the mortgagee to spend, and later the mortgagee can detain him for reimbursement. Expenses related to preserving or returning the pawn to the mortgagee's hand (e.g., treatment of wounds, guard's wage, storage rent) are on the mortgagee. Abu Yusuf differs on housing rent, placing it on the mortgagor. The reward for recovering a runaway slave (ja'l al-abiq) is on the mortgagee if the pawn was wholly liable, but apportioned if part is trust. The land tax (kharaj) is always on the mortgagor. The 'ushr (tithe) on produce takes precedence over the mortgagee's right.

Key Terms
أجرة بيت الحفظ (ujrat bayt al-hifz): Rent of the storage house where the pawn is kept.
خراج (kharaj): Land tax imposed on agricultural land.
نفقة (nafaqah): Upkeep or maintenance expenses for the pawn, such as food for animals.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Who bears the cost of treating a wound on the pawn?
A) The mortgagor (owner) because the pawn is his property.
B) The mortgagee (creditor) because it is part of preservation and returning the pawn.
C) Both equally because it benefits both.
D) The judge if the owner refuses.

Key Takeaway
The allocation of pawn expenses depends on purpose: expenses for preservation and return are on the mortgagee; expenses for the pawn's own benefit and maintenance are on the mortgagor.

Allocation of Expenses in Rahn: The Principle and Its Applications
Explaining the legal principles governing liability for expenses in a pledge contract

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between preservation costs (on the mortgagee) and maintenance costs (on the mortgagor) and apply the rule to specific scenarios.

Core Idea
Preservation costs on mortgagee; maintenance costs on mortgagor. The pledge remains the mortgagor's property.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq expands on the core rule of expenses in a pledge contract. The mortgagee must preserve the pledge, so costs of preservation (storage, guard) fall on him. The mortgagor retains ownership, so ongoing upkeep (feed, wages, taxes) is his responsibility. We will explore the principle, its evidence, and its practical applications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the General Rule
The text opens with a clear statement: preservation and its guardian are on the mortgagee; the shepherd's wage, maintenance, and land tax are on the mortgagor. Note the two categories: preservation (hifz) versus maintenance (nafaqa).
This is the foundational principle that governs all subsequent examples.
2
Understand the Principle
The author provides the underlying rationale: expenses for the benefit and preservation of the pledge itself are on the mortgagor (as owner), while expenses for custody and returning the pledge to the mortgagee’s possession are on the mortgagee (as custodian).
Example: food for an animal is maintenance; a guard’s wage is preservation.
3
Apply to Examples
Trace specific examples: food, drink, shepherd’s wage, slave clothing, wet nurse wage, canal digging, watering, pollinating, harvesting fall under maintenance (mortgagor). Medical treatment, shelter rent, guard’s wage fall under preservation (mortgagee).
The rule is consistent: if the expense is to preserve the asset for the mortgagee's security, it's on the mortgagee.
4
Note the Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Abu Yusuf disagrees on shelter rent (ajr al-ma'wa), considering it like maintenance and thus on the mortgagor. The majority holds it is preservation and on the mortgagee.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf within the school; the mufta bihi is the majority view.
5
Consider Special Cases
The passage also covers emergency sale, recovery of runaway slaves (jual), and the priority of 'ushr over the mortgagee's right. These follow the same principle but require additional fiqh reasoning.
Understanding these edge cases solidifies the principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الرهن
الحفظ وحافظه على المرتهن وأجرة راعيه ونفقته والخراج على الراهن ولا يجوز رهن الاستعمال بغير إذن المالك، وكذا الطيلسان إن لبسه لبساً معتاداً ضمن، ولو وضعه على عنقه لم يضمن. وفي الواقعات رجل رهن عند رجل خاتماً وقال للمرتهن تختم به إن أمره أن يتختم به في الخنصر فهلك في حال التختم بهلك بالدين لأنه أمر بالحفظ لا بالاستعمال. وفي الذخيرة هو الصحيح. ولو رهنه سيفين فتلقدهما ضمن قال فخر الدين: والفتوى على أنه يضمن، وفي الثلاثة لا يضمن لأن العادة جرت بين الشجعان بتقليد السيفين في الحرب دون الثلاثة. وفي المحيط : ولو باع المرتهن زوائد الرهن بغير إذن الراهن أو القاضي لم يجز بيعه ويضمن قيمته، وإن خاف تلفه فجذ الثمار وحلب اللبن جاز استحساناً لأنه نوع من الحفظ، فإن خاف تلفه عنده فأمسكه يرفع الأمر إلى القاضي حتى يبيعه أو يأذن له في البيع إن كان المالك غائباً، وإن كان حاضراً يرجع إليه، ولو كان المرتهن بعيداً من القاضي والمالك وخاف التلف فباعه بنفسه لم يضمن هكذا روي عن محمد لأنه مأذون له في مثل هذه الحالة في البيع دلالة وليس للمرتهن ولا للراهن أن يزرع الأرض ولا أن يؤجرها لأنه ليس له الانتفاع بالرهن. قال رحمه الله : ( وأجرة بيت الحفظ وحافظه على المرتهن وأجرة راعيه ونفقته والخراج على الراهن والأصل فيه أن ما يحتاج إليه لمصلحة الرهن لنفسه وتبقيته فهو على الراهن، سواء كان في فصل أو لم يكن لأن العين باقية على ملكه وكذا منافعه مملوكة له فيكون أصلاً وتبقيته عليه لما أنه مؤنة ملكه كما في الوديعة وذلك مثل النفقة مأكله ومشربه وأجرة الراعي مثله لأنه علف البهائم، ومن هذا الجنس كسوة الرقيق وأجرة ظئر ولد الرهن وكري النهر وكسر النهر وسقي البساتين وتلقيح نخيله وجذاذها والقيام بمصالحه. وفي النوازل : أبي الراهن أن ينفق على الرهن فالقاضي يأمر المرتهن بالنفقة، فإذا قبض الدين فللمرتهن أن يحبسه على النفقة فإن هلك في هذه الحالة فالنفقة على الراهن وكل ما كان لحفظه أو لرده إلى يد المرتهن أو لرد جزء منه كمداواة الجرح فهو على المرتهن مثل أجرة الحافظ لأن الإمساك حق له والحفظ واجب عليه فتكون مؤنته عليه، وكذلك أجرة البيت التي يحفظ فيه الرهن. وعن أبي يوسف أن أجرة المأوى على الراهن بمنزلة النفقة، ومن هذا القسم جعل الآبق إذا كان كله مضموناً لأن يد الاستيفاء كانت ثابتة على المحل ويحتاج إلى إعادة يد الاستيفاء ليرده على المالك فكانت من مؤنة الرد فتكون عليه. وإن كان بعضه أمانة فيقدر المضمون على المرتهن وحصة الأمانة على الراهن ولأن الرد لإعادة اليد ويده في الزيادة يد المالك إذ هو كالمودع فيها فتكون على المالك بخلاف أجرة البيت الذي يحفظ فيه الرهن فإن كلها تجب على المرتهن كيفما كان لأن وجوبها لأجل الحبس وحق الحبس ثابت له في الكل. وأما الجعل فلأجل الضمان فيتقدر بقدره، والمداواة والفداء من الجناية ينقسم على المضمون والأمانة والخراج على الراهن لأنه مؤنة الملك والعشر فيما يخرج مقدم على حق المرتهن لتعلقه بالعين ولا يبطل الرهن به في الباقي لأن وجوبه لا ينافي ملكه ألا ترى أنه لو باع الخارج كله في غير الرهن قبل أداء العشر يجوز فكذا له أن يخرج بدل العشر من مال آخر، وإن كان ملكه ثابتاً فيه بقي رهناً على حاله بخلاف استحقاق جزء شائع من الرهن حيث يبطل الرهن في الباقي لأنه تبين بالاستحقاق أنه لا يملك قدر المستحق فكان الرهن شائعاً من الابتداء، وتبين أن الرهن كان باطلاً ولا كذلك وجوب العشر لأن وجوبه لا ينافي ملك الراهن لا فيه ولا في غيره. ثم إذا خرج منه العشر خرج ذلك الجزء عن ملكه في الوقت فلم يوجب شيوعاً في الباقي لا طارئاً ولا مقارناً وما أداه أحدهما مما يجب على الآخر بغير أمر القاضي فهو متطوع كما إذا قضى دين غيره بغير أمره وإن كان بأمر القاضي وجعله ديناً على الآخر رجع عليه، وبمجرد أمر القاضي من غير تصريح بجعله ديناً عليه لا يرجع عليه إذا كان صاحبه حاضراً، وإن كان بأمر القاضي لأنه يمكنه أن يرفع الأمر إلى القاضي فيأمر صاحبه بذلك. وقال أبو يوسف رحمه الله تعالى : يرجع في الوجهين وهو فرع مسألة الحجر لأن القاضي لا يلي على الحاضر ولا ينفذ أمره عليه. وفي المحيط : والعشر والخراج على الراهن .اهـ.
The Book of Pledge. Preservation and its guardian are upon the mortgagee, and the wage of its shepherd, its maintenance, and the land tax are upon the mortgagor. The mortgagee may not use the pledge without the owner's permission. Similarly, the taylasan: if he wears it in a customary manner, he is liable; if he places it on his neck, he is not liable. In al-Waqi'at: a man pledged a ring and said to the mortgagee, 'Wear it.' If he ordered him to wear it on the little finger and it perished while wearing, it perishes from the debt because he ordered preservation, not use. In al-Dhakhira: it is correct. If he pledged two swords and girded both, he is liable. Fakhr al-Din said: The fatwa is that he is liable. For three, he is not liable because the custom among brave men is to gird two swords in war, not three. In al-Muhit: If the mortgagee sold excess produce without permission, the sale is invalid and he is liable for its value. If he feared loss and cut fruits or milked, it is permissible by istihsan as preservation. If he feared loss and kept it, he raises the matter to the judge to sell or authorize sale if owner absent. If present, he refers to him. If the mortgagee is far from judge and owner and fears loss, he may sell without liability, as narrated from Muhammad. The mortgagee and mortgagor may not cultivate or lease the land. He said: The rent of storage and its guardian are on the mortgagee, the shepherd's wage, maintenance, and kharaj on the mortgagor. The principle: what is needed for the benefit and preservation of the pledge is on the mortgagor, whether in season or not, because the asset remains his property. Examples: food, drink, shepherd's wage, slave clothing, wet nurse wage, digging canals, watering, pollinating, harvesting. In al-Nawazil: If the mortgagor refuses to spend, the judge orders the mortgagee to spend. When he collects the debt, he may detain for expenses. If it perishes, expenses are on the mortgagor. Preservation and return costs (e.g., medical treatment) are on the mortgagee, as his right is detention. Abu Yusuf: shelter rent is on the mortgagor. Reward for recovering a runaway slave is apportioned. Kharaj is on the mortgagor. 'Ushr takes priority over the mortgagee's right. Payment by one on behalf of the other without judge's order is voluntary. If by judge's order and made a debt, he can reclaim. Abu Yusuf: he can reclaim in both cases. In al-Muhit: 'Ushr and kharaj are on the mortgagor.
Source pages p. 441

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is an expansion on the rules of expenses in pledge (rahn). The author begins by stating a general rule: preservation costs (e.g., wages of a guard, rent of storage) are on the mortgagee, while the upkeep of the asset itself (e.g., shepherd's wage, feed, kharaj tax) is on the mortgagor. He then provides detailed examples and exceptions. The core principle is that expenses for the benefit and preservation of the pledged item itself fall on the owner (mortgagor), while expenses incurred for custody and returning the item to the mortgagee's possession fall on the mortgagee. The text also addresses the mortgagee's use of the pledge: unauthorized use leads to liability, but customary use (like wearing a garment) may differ. The mortgagee's sale of excess produce without permission is invalid unless done out of necessity (istihsan). The mortgagee may act to preserve the pledge in emergencies. The passage then elaborates on the allocation of costs for medical treatment, recovery of escaped slaves, and the priority of 'ushr and kharaj over the mortgagee's right. The discussion concludes with the issue of voluntary payment by one party on behalf of the other and the role of the judge.

Key Terms
الرهن (al-rahn): A contract of pledge where property is deposited as security for a debt.
المرتهن (al-murtahin): The mortgagee, the creditor who holds the pledge.
الراهن (al-rahin): The mortgagor, the debtor who gives the pledge.
العشر (al-'ushr): A tithe of one-tenth on agricultural produce, an Islamic tax.
الخراج (al-kharaj): A land tax on kharaj lands (conquered lands remaining in non-Muslim hands).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Who bears the cost of medical treatment for a wound on a pledged animal?
A) The mortgagor, because he owns the animal
B) The mortgagee, because it is part of preservation
C) They split the cost equally
D) The judge pays from public funds

Key Takeaway
In a pledge, preservation costs (e.g., storage, guard, medical treatment) are on the mortgagee, while maintenance costs (e.g., feed, wages, tax) are on the mortgagor, following the principle that the pledge's benefit belongs to the owner.

Evidence in Pledge Disputes: The Hanafi Preference Rules
Expanding on evidence rules in pledge disputes from al-Mabsut

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule for accepting evidence in a pledge dispute and apply it to scenarios where property is present or destroyed.

Core Idea
The mortgagee's evidence prevails when property exists; the mortgagor's when property is destroyed and he claims more.

This lesson unpacks the Hanafi rules for resolving conflicting claims in a pledge (rahn). The key is whose evidence benefits whom and the status of the property. We'll see how the school distinguishes between possession and value, and how denial by silence triggers liability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute scenario
Determine whether the pledged property is still in the mortgagee's hand or has been destroyed. This dictates which party's evidence is preferred.
2
Determine whose evidence benefits whom
The mortgagee's evidence that the property was pledged as claimed establishes a right for himself (possession). The mortgagor's evidence would only benefit the mortgagee (by confirming the pledge). Evidence that benefits the witness himself is preferred.
Hanafi principle: 'Bayanat al-murtahin tuthbit al-haqq li nafsihi'
3
Apply the preference rule
If property exists: mortgagee's evidence accepted. If property is destroyed: mortgagor's evidence is accepted if he claims a higher value than the mortgagee admits, because his evidence establishes an increase in the debt or value.
4
Understand silence as denial
If the mortgagee remains silent when the mortgagor claims the property was worth more, silence is legally equivalent to denial, making the mortgagee a guarantor for the difference. This is analogous to a depositary who denies a deposit.
Analogy (qiyas) to al-mudi' (depositary) who denies the deposit.
5
Consider the presumptive value
When the current value is known, it can serve as evidence for the value at the time of the contract. The present is a witness for the past, as in the rental example.
Text: 'فات الحال شاهداً للماضي'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولم يذكر المؤلف الدعوى والشهادة في الرهن ودعوى الرجلين الرهن أو أحدهما . قال في المبسوط مسائله على فصول: فصل في اختلافهما في الرهن وفصل في اختلاف الشاهدين في النطق وفصل في شهادة الراهنين والمرتهنين بالمرهون لغيره وفصل في إقامة الواحد البينة على رجلين في الرهن . قال الراهن رهنتك هذه العين وقبضتها مني وأقام البينة والعين قائمة في يد المرتهن وهو ينكر أو قال بل رهنتني عيناً أخرى فأقاما البينة تقبل بينة المرتهن والقول له ولا تقبل بينة الراهن لأن بينة المرتهن تثبت الحق لنفسه وبينة الراهن تثبت الحق لغيره وهو ملك اليد والحبس وبينة من يثبت الحق لنفسه أولى ولأنه لا فائدة في قبول بينة الراهن لأن المرتهن رد ذلك فإن الرهن غير لازم. وإذا كانت العين هالكة فالبينة للراهن إذا كان ما يدعيه الراهن أكثر لأن بينته تثبت زيادة. أقام الراهن البيئة أنه رهنه عبداً بألف يساوي ألفين وقبضه وأنكر المرتهن يضمن قيمته كلها النصف يسقط بدينه ويؤخذ بالنصف لأنه جحده فصار ضامناً بالجحود كالمودع جحد الوديعة يصير ضامناً للوديعة، وكذلك إن سكت المرتهن ولم يقر ولم يجحد لأن السكوت جحود حكماً ألا ترى لو أخر شيئاً فسكت يسمع عليه كما لو جحد . ولو قال المرتهن تساوي خمسمائة لا يسمع قوله لأنه خلاف ما قامت عليه، ولو قال المرتهن رهنتني هذين الثوبين وقال الراهن أحدهما بعينه فالقول للراهن والبينة للمرتهن لأن المرتهن يدعي عليه زيادة رهن وهو ينكر الرهن. رهن عبداً والدين ألف فذهب عين العبد وهو يساوي ألفاً فقال الراهن كانت هذه قيمته يوم رهنتك فقد ذهب نصف حقك وقال المرتهن بل كانت خمسمائة يومئذ وإزدادت من بعد القول للراهن والبينة له أيضاً لأن القيمة للحال ألف فيكون الحال شاهداً للماضي كمن استأجر طاحونة واختلفا في ضامناً
The author did not mention litigation and testimony in pledge, nor the claim of two men regarding the pledge or one of them. He said in al-Mabsut: Its issues are in chapters: Chapter on their disagreement in pledge, Chapter on disagreement of two witnesses in wording, Chapter on testimony of mortgagors and mortgagees about the pledged property for someone else, Chapter on one person presenting evidence against two in pledge. The mortgagor says: 'I pledged this asset to you and you took possession of it from me,' and he brings evidence while the asset is in the mortgagee's hand and he denies, or he says, 'Rather, you pledged to me another asset,' and both bring evidence. The mortgagee's evidence is accepted and his word is taken, and the mortgagor's evidence is not accepted because the mortgagee's evidence establishes a right for himself, while the mortgagor's evidence establishes a right for another (the right of possession and detention). Evidence establishing a right for oneself is preferred. Also, there is no benefit in accepting the mortgagor's evidence because the mortgagee rejected it, since the pledge is not binding. If the asset is destroyed, the evidence is for the mortgagor if what he claims is more, because his evidence establishes an increase. If the mortgagor brings evidence that he pledged a slave worth two thousand for one thousand and took possession, and the mortgagee denies, he is liable for its full value, with half falling due to the debt and he is taken for the half because he denied, so he becomes a guarantor by denial, like a depositary who denies a deposit becomes liable for the deposit. Similarly, if the mortgagee remains silent without affirmance or denial, because silence is legally equivalent to denial. If the mortgagee says, 'It is worth five hundred,' his statement is not heard because it contradicts what was established. If the mortgagee says, 'You pledged these two garments to me,' and the mortgagor says, 'One specific one,' the word is of the mortgagor and the evidence is for the mortgagee because the mortgagee claims an additional pledge while he denies the pledge. [Scenario:] A slave is pledged and the debt is one thousand, then the slave perishes, and he is worth one thousand. The mortgagor says, 'This was its value on the day I pledged it, so half your right is gone.' The mortgagee says, 'Rather, it was worth five hundred then and increased afterwards.' The word is of the mortgagor, and the evidence is also for him because the current value is one thousand, so the present testifies to the past, like one who rents a mill and they disagree about...
Source pages p. 442

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq deals with rules of evidence when there is a dispute in a pledge contract. The author draws from al-Mabsut and presents several scenarios. The key principle: when the pledged property is physically in the mortgagee's possession, his claim is given precedence because his testimony supports his own right. The mortgagor's testimony is not accepted if the property exists because it would only benefit the mortgagee. However, if the property is destroyed, the mortgagor's evidence is preferred if he claims a higher value, because his evidence establishes an increase in the debt or value. The text also addresses the liability of the mortgagee for denial by silence, equating silence with denial in legal effect. It further discusses scenarios where the mortgagee denies the pledge's value, and where there is disagreement over the number of items pledged. The ruling emphasizes that the current market value can be used as evidence for the value at the time of the contract.

Key Terms
البينة (al-bayyinah): Clear evidence, typically the testimony of two witnesses or equivalent established proof.
الشهادة (al-shahadah): Testimony before a judge to establish a claim.
الراهن (al-rahin): The mortgagor, the debtor who gives the pledge.
المرتهن (al-murtahin): The mortgagee, the creditor who holds the pledge.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The mortgagor's, because he is the owner
B) The mortgagee's, because his evidence benefits himself
C) Both are rejected
D) The court requires an oath from both

Key Takeaway
In pledge disputes, the evidence that benefits the party himself is preferred; the destruction of property shifts the preference to the mortgagor for claiming higher value.

Effect of 'Ushr and Kharaj on Mortgage and Dispute on Recovery by Court Order
Intra-Hanafi dispute on mortgage validity and recovery

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the validity of a mortgage despite the obligation of 'ushr, and to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf regarding recovery of amounts paid by qadi's order.

Core Idea
The obligation of 'ushr does not invalidate a mortgage; recovery by court order requires explicit debt creation per Abu Hanifa.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq clarifies that the tithe ('ushr) on agricultural produce does not affect the validity of a mortgage because the obligation does not negate ownership. It then delves into an intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf on whether a person who pays the 'ushr on behalf of another by court order can recover the amount. Understanding this dispute is crucial for correctly applying Hanafi rulings in financial transactions involving mortgages and land taxes.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi ruling on mortgage validity with 'ushr
Identify that the obligation of 'ushr does not invalidate the mortgage because it does not contradict the mortgagor's ownership.
The author distinguishes this from a claim of a common share in the mortgage, which would void it.
2
Analyze the principle: obligation does not negate ownership
Understand that the obligation to pay 'ushr does not mean the mortgaged property is not owned; the mortgagor can pay from other wealth.
The analogy with selling all produce before paying 'ushr supports this.
3
Distinguish between payment without and with court order
If someone pays without qadi's order, it is voluntary (no recovery). If with order, recovery depends on whether the qadi explicitly made it a debt.
4
Analyze the dispute between Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf
Abu Hanifa says no recovery if the obligor is present and the qadi did not explicitly create a debt; Abu Yusuf says recovery in both cases based on the analogy of interdiction.
5
Determine the mufta bihi position
The relied-upon position is Abu Hanifa's view: recovery requires explicit debt creation.
The author presents Abu Hanifa's view first and more prominently.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بالعين ولا يبطل الرهن به في الباقي لأن وجوبه لا ينافي ملكه ألا ترى أنه لو باع الخارج كله في غير الرهن قبل أداء العشر يجوز فكذا له أن يخرج بدل العشر من مال آخر، وإن كان ملكه ثابتاً فيه بقي رهناً على حاله بخلاف استحقاق جزء شائع من الرهن حيث يبطل الرهن في الباقي لأنه تبين بالاستحقاق أنه لا يملك قدر المستحق فكان الرهن شائعاً من الابتداء ، وتبين أن الرهن كان باطلاً ولا كذلك وجوب العشر لأن وجوبه لا ينافي ملك الراهن لا فيه ولا في غيره. ثم إذا خرج منه العشر خرج ذلك الجزء عن ملكه في الوقت فلم يوجب شيوعاً في الباقي لا طارئاً ولا مقارناً وما أداه أحدهما مما يجب على الآخر بغير أمر القاضي فهو متطوع كما إذا قضى دين غيره بغير أمره وإن كان بأمر القاضي وجعله ديناً على الآخر رجع عليه، وبمجرد أمر القاضي من غير تصريح بجعله ديناً عليه لا يرجع عليه إذا كان صاحبه حاضراً، وإن كان بأمر القاضي لأنه يمكنه أن يرفع الأمر إلى القاضي فيأمر صاحبه بذلك. وقال أبو يوسف رحمه الله تعالى : يرجع في الوجهين وهو فرع مسألة الحجر لأن القاضي لا يلي على الحاضر ولا ينفذ أمره عليه . وفي المحيط : والعشر والخراج على الراهن .اهـ.
With the principal, and the mortgage is not invalidated regarding the rest, because its obligation does not contradict his ownership. Do you not see that if he sold the entire product outside the mortgage before paying the tithe, it is permissible; likewise, he may substitute the tithe from other wealth. And if his ownership remains in it, it remains mortgaged as it was, unlike the case of claiming a common share in the mortgage, where the mortgage is voided regarding the rest, because it becomes clear by the claim that he does not own the claimed amount, so the mortgage was common from the beginning, and it becomes clear that the mortgage was void. Not so with the obligation of tithe, because its obligation does not contradict the mortgagor's ownership neither in it nor outside it. Then if he pays the tithe from it, that part leaves his ownership at the time, but does not cause commonality in the rest, neither subsequently nor initially. And whoever pays what is due on another without the qadi's order is a volunteer, just as if he paid another's debt without his order. If it was by the qadi's order and he made it a debt on the other, he may recover. But with merely the qadi's order without explicitly making it a debt on him, he does not recover when the other party is present, even if it was by the qadi's order, because he could have raised the matter to the qadi who would order the other party to do it. Abu Yusuf said: he recovers in both cases, and it is a branch of the issue of interdiction, because the qadi has no authority over the present person and his order does not apply to him. In al-Muhit: The tithe and kharaj are upon the mortgagor.
Source pages p. 442

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof and an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the effect of 'ushr (tithe) and kharaj (land tax) on a mortgaged property. The author begins by stating that the obligation of 'ushr does not conflict with the mortgagor's ownership, so the mortgage remains valid in the rest of the property. He draws an analogy: just as the mortgagor can sell the entire produce from the land before paying the tithe, he can also substitute the tithe from other wealth. If the tithe is paid from the mortgaged property itself, that portion leaves his ownership temporarily, but it does not create a common share in the remaining mortgaged property, because the tithe obligation does not negate ownership. The author distinguishes this from the case where a common share in the mortgaged property is claimed by a third party, which voids the mortgage because it reveals that the mortgagor never owned that share. Then the passage discusses recovery of the tithe paid by one party on behalf of the other. If someone pays what is due on another without a qadi's order, it is a voluntary payment. If the qadi orders the payment and explicitly makes it a debt against the other, he can recover. But if the qadi merely orders without explicit debt creation, and the actual obligor is present, the payer cannot recover because the obligor could have been ordered directly. Abu Yusuf disagrees: he says the payer recovers in both cases, arguing that the qadi's order overrides the authority over the present person, similar to the case of interdiction where the qadi can appoint a guardian even over a present person. The passage concludes by quoting al-Muhit that 'ushr and kharaj are on the mortgagor, confirming the earlier ruling.

Key Terms
العشر (al-'ushr): The tithe (one-tenth) due on agricultural produce, a kind of zakat.
الرهن (al-rahn): A pledge or mortgage where property is held as security for a debt.
القاضي (al-qadi): The judge who has authority to order payments and create debts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, when can a person recover the tithe they paid on behalf of another by court order?
A) In all cases if the court ordered the payment.
B) Only if the court explicitly made it a debt against the obligor.
C) Only if the obligor was absent.
D) Never, because it is a voluntary payment.

Key Takeaway
The obligation of 'ushr does not invalidate a mortgage, and recovery of amounts paid by court order is only possible when the qadi explicitly creates a debt on the obligor, per the preferred Hanafi view.

Burden of Proof in Mortgage Dispute – Property Extant in Mortgagee's Hands
Ruling on evidence preference in mortgage dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on whose evidence is preferred when mortgagor and mortgagee dispute over the pledged property still in the mortgagee's possession, and explain the two reasons given for preferring the mortgagee's evidence.

Core Idea
When the property is still with the mortgagee, his evidence is preferred because it establishes his own right to possession, while the mortgagor's evidence only benefits another.

This passage from al-Mabsut, quoted by Ibn Nujaym in al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, addresses a procedural fiqh question: if the mortgagor and mortgagee disagree about the identity of the pledged property, and the property is still in the mortgagee's hands, whose evidence should be accepted? The Hanafi position, grounded in a reasoning from benefit (maslahah) and the non-binding nature of mortgage on the mortgagee, gives priority to the mortgagee's evidence. Let's unpack the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute scenario
The passage presents two scenarios: (a) The mortgagor claims he pledged a specific property and the mortgagee took possession, but the mortgagee denies; (b) Both claim different properties were pledged. In both, the property exists in the mortgagee's hands and both bring evidence.
2
State the Hanafi ruling
The mortgagee's evidence is accepted and his statement is taken; the mortgagor's evidence is not accepted.
3
Follow the first reason
The mortgagee's evidence establishes a right for himself (the right to hold the property as security, i.e., malk al-yad wa al-habs). The mortgagor's evidence establishes a right for another (the mortgagee). Evidence that proves a right for oneself is more deserving (awla) than evidence that proves a right for another.
This is a general principle in Hanafi law of evidence.
4
Follow the second reason
There is no benefit (fa'idah) in accepting the mortgagor's evidence because even if he proves his claim, the mortgagee could simply renounce the mortgage, as the mortgage is not binding (ghayr lazim) on the mortgagee. Thus, accepting the mortgagor's evidence would not lead to a practical resolution.
The non-binding nature of rahn on the mortgagee is a key Hanafi rule.
5
Note the underlying maxim
The ruling reflects the maxim: 'Bayyinah li man yuthbit al-haqq li nafsihi' (Evidence is for the one who proves a right for himself).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولم يذكر المؤلف الدعوى والشهادة في الرهن ودعوى الرجلين الرهن أو أحدهما . قال في المبسوط مسائله على فصول: فصل في اختلافهما في الرهن وفصل في اختلاف الشاهدين في النطق وفصل في شهادة الراهنين والمرتهنين بالمرهون لغيره وفصل في إقامة الواحد البينة على رجلين في الرهن . قال الراهن رهنتك هذه العين وقبضتها مني وأقام البينة والعين قائمة في يد المرتهن وهو ينكر أو قال بل رهنتني عيناً أخرى فأقاما البينة تقبل بينة المرتهن والقول له ولا تقبل بينة الراهن لأن بينة المرتهن تثبت الحق لنفسه وبينة الراهن تثبت الحق لغيره وهو ملك اليد والحبس وبينة من يثبت الحق لنفسه أولى ولأنه لا فائدة في قبول بينة الراهن لأن المرتهن رد ذلك فإن الرهن غير لازم.
The author did not mention claims and testimony in mortgage, and the claim of two men for mortgage or one of them. He said in al-Mabsut: its issues are in chapters: Chapter on their dispute in mortgage, Chapter on dispute of two witnesses in wording, Chapter on testimony of the two mortgagors and mortgagees regarding the mortgaged property belonging to another, Chapter on one person establishing evidence against two in mortgage. He said: The mortgagor said: I pledged this specific property to you and you took possession from me, and he brought evidence while the property is extant in the hand of the mortgagee and he denies, or said: you pledged another property to me, and both brought evidence – the evidence of the mortgagee is accepted and his statement is taken, and the evidence of the mortgagor is not accepted, because the mortgagee's evidence establishes right for himself, while the mortgagor's evidence establishes right for another, which is the right of possession and retention. The evidence of one who establishes right for himself is more deserving. And because there is no benefit in accepting the mortgagor's evidence since the mortgagee rejected it, for the mortgage is not binding.
Source pages p. 442

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on evidence in mortgage disputes when the property is still in the mortgagee's hands. The author introduces the topic by summarizing that the original text did not cover claims and testimony in mortgage. He then quotes al-Mabsut which divides the subject into several chapters. The specific ruling here: If the mortgagor claims he pledged a specific property and the mortgagee denies or claims a different property, and both bring evidence, the mortgagee's evidence is accepted and his statement is given weight. The mortar's evidence is rejected for two reasons: (1) The mortgagee's evidence establishes a right for himself (right of possession and retention), while the mortgagor's evidence establishes a right for another (the mortgagee's right). Evidence that benefits oneself is stronger. (2) There is no benefit in accepting the mortgagor's evidence because the mortgagee has denied it, and the mortgage is not a binding contract on the mortgagee (he can renounce it). Thus, accepting the mortgagee's evidence settles the dispute practically.

Key Terms
البينة (al-bayyinah): Evidence or clear proof, usually two witnesses or strong circumstantial evidence.
الرهن غير لازم (al-rahn ghayr lazim): The mortgage is not binding on the mortgagee; he can cancel it.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the mortgagee's evidence preferred over the mortgagor's when the property is extant?
A) Because the mortgagor is the owner.
B) Because the mortgage is a binding contract.
C) Because the mortgagee's evidence establishes a right for himself.
D) Because the mortgagee's testimony is stronger.

Key Takeaway
In mortgage disputes where the property is in the mortgagee's hands, the Hanafi school gives evidentiary priority to the mortgagee because his evidence secures his own interest and there is no practical gain in accepting the mortgagor's evidence due to the non-binding nature of the mortgage.
