Pledge for a Promised Debt and Its Rulings
Discusses validity of a pledge for a future debt and the consequences if the pledge is lost.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on a pledge for a promised debt and apply the rule of guarantee by the lesser value.

Core Idea
A pledge for a future debt is valid; the promise is treated as if the debt already exists.

This lesson examines a unique Hanafi ruling: a pledge given before the debt exists is valid due to necessity. The passage explains the legal reasoning, the guarantee upon loss, and the intra-madhhab differences. By the end, you will understand how the Hanafi school reconciles the need for collateral with the principle that a pledge follows a debt.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
Determine what is being ruled upon: a pledge given for a future loan. The Hanafi ruling is that it is valid (sahih).
Contrast with the invalid pledge for damages discussed earlier.
2
Locate the legal reasoning (ta'lil)
The author uses the principle 'al-maw'ud ka al-mawjud' – the promise is treated as existent. This is based on istihsan (juristic preference) to fulfill practical needs.
This is the core dalil for validity.
3
Determine the guarantee upon loss
If the pledge is destroyed while with the pledgee, the debt is reduced to the lesser of the pledge's value and the promised amount. This is because the pledge is guaranteed by the debt.
If the value is lower, the debt reduces; if the debt is lower, the pledge covers only that amount.
4
Analyze the unstated amount case
If no amount was named, the pledgee determines the debt upon destruction, akin to a confession. Abu Yusuf holds that the full value of the pledge is owed.
This shows different applications of the principle.
5
Compare with similar concepts
The text contrasts with 'maqbood 'ala sawm al-shira' (item taken on purchase negotiation), which is guaranteed by its full value because it is guaranteed by itself, not by an external debt.
This distinction is key to understanding the Hanafi position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو قبض الرهن بالدرك قبل الوجوب بالاستحقاق فهلك عند المشتري يهلك أمانة لأنه لا عقد حينئذ فوقع باطلاً بخلاف الرهن بالدين الموعود وهو أن يقول رهنتك هذا بألف لتقرضني وهلك في يد المرتهن حيث يهلك ما سمى من المال لأن الموعود جعل كالموجود باعتبار الحاجة بل جعل موجوداً اقتضاء لأن الرهن استيفاء والاستيفاء لا يسبق الوجوب بل يتلوه، ولا بد من سبق الوجود ليكون الاستيفاء مسبباً عليه ولأنه مقبوض بجهة الرهن الذي يصح على اعتبار وجوده فيعطى له حكمه كالمقبوض على سوم الشراء فيكون مضموناً عليه بالأقل مما سمى، ومن قيمة الرهن إذا سمى قدر الموعود، وإن لم يسم قدره بأن رهنه على أن يعطيه شيئاً فهلك الرهن في يده يعطي المرتهن الراهن ما شاه لأنه بالهلاك صار مستوفياً شيئاً فيكون بيانه إليه كما لو أقر بذلك. وعن أبي يوسف : لو قال أقرضني وخذ هذا رهناً ولم يسم . شيئاً وهلك يضمن قيمة الرهن بخلاف المقبوض على سوم الشراء حيث يجب على القابض جميع قيمته لأنه مضمون بنفسه كالبيع الفاسد والمغصوب فلا يتقدر بغيره ولا كذلك الرهن فإنه مضمون بغيره وهو الدين فيكون مقدراً به، وروى المعلي عن أبي يوسف أنه تجب قيمة الرهن في الدين الموعود بالغة ما بلغت كالمقبوض على سوم الشراء،
And if he takes possession of the pledge for damages (darak) before the obligation by revocation, and it is destroyed with the buyer, it is destroyed as a trust because there is no contract at that time, so it occurred invalid, unlike the pledge for a promised debt, which is when he says, 'I pledge this to you for a thousand to lend me,' and it is destroyed in the hand of the pledgee, where it is destroyed for the amount named of money because the promised is made like the existing out of necessity; rather it is made existent as a requisite because the pledge is collection, and collection does not precede obligation but follows it, and there must be a prior existence for collection to be based on it, and because it is taken possession of by way of a pledge that is valid on the assumption of its existence, so it is given its ruling like that taken on the basis of a purchase negotiation (sawm al-shira), so it is guaranteed by the lesser of what was named and the value of the pledge if he named the amount of the promise. And if he did not name its amount, such as by pledging it for him to give him something, and the pledge is destroyed in his hand, the pledgee gives the pledgor what he wants because by the destruction he became someone who has collected something, so its explanation is left to him as if he confessed to that. And from Abu Yusuf: If he said 'Lend me and take this as a pledge' and did not name anything and it is destroyed, he guarantees the value of the pledge, unlike that taken on the basis of a purchase negotiation where the taker owes the entire value because it is guaranteed by itself like a void sale and usurped item, so it is not measured by other; and not so the pledge, because it is guaranteed by other, which is the debt, so it is measured by it. And from al-Mu'alla from Abu Yusuf: the value of the pledge is owed in the promised debt whatever it reaches, like that taken on the basis of a purchase negotiation.
Source pages p. 449

Commentary
This passage addresses the ruling of a pledge given for a future (promised) debt, such as when someone says, 'Take this pledge now and I will lend you the money later.' The author explains that this is valid, unlike the pledge for damages discussed earlier. The key legal reasoning is that the promise is treated as if the debt already exists (ja'l al-maw'ud ka al-mawjud) due to necessity, because the pledge is a form of collection (istifa') that must follow the obligation. Therefore, the pledge is considered in the debtor's possession and is guaranteed. If the pledge is destroyed, the pledgee loses his right to the debt to the extent of the lesser of the pledged item's value or the promised amount. If the amount was not specified, the pledgee decides the value at destruction, akin to a confession. A difference of opinion from Abu Yusuf is mentioned: if no amount was named, the entire value of the pledge is guaranteed, compared to a down payment on a purchase (sawm al-shira). Another narration from Abu Yusuf (from al-Mu'alla) states the full value is owed regardless, similar to the purchase negotiation case. This intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf shows variation in applying the principle.

Key Terms
الدين الموعود (al-dayn al-maw'ud): A debt that is promised to be lent in the future; the pledge is given before the debt is created.
مقبوض على سوم الشراء (maqbood 'ala sawm al-shira): An item taken into possession with the intention of purchase, where the price is not yet fixed; it is guaranteed by its full value.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What happens if a pledge given for a promised debt of 100 dirhams is destroyed when its value is 80 dirhams?
A) The pledgee is entitled to 80 dirhams from the pledgor.
B) The pledgee loses the entire debt.
C) The pledgor must pay the full 100 dirhams.
D) The pledgee must return the value of the pledge to the pledgor.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a pledge for a promised debt is valid and upon loss, the pledgee's claim is reduced to the lesser of the pledge's value and the promised amount.

Pledge with the Sold Item is Invalid
Ruling with proof for invalidity of pledge with sold item.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a sold item cannot be used as a pledge and explain the reasoning based on guarantee.

Core Idea
A sold item is guaranteed by the price, so it cannot be pledged for another debt.

This passage begins by addressing a specific question: can a buyer who has taken possession of a sold item but not yet paid the price use that very item as a pledge for another debt? The Hanafi school rules that this is invalid. The reason is that the sold item is already 'guaranteed' (madhmun) by the price, so it cannot serve as security for a separate obligation. We will examine the textual reasoning and the practical consequence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
The act is using a sold item (already in buyer's possession) as a pledge. The ruling is invalid (batil).
2
Locate the dalil
The text states: 'because it is guaranteed by other, because it is guaranteed by the price.'
This is a reasoning based on the nature of the sold item.
3
Understand the illa
The sold item is already a security for the price, so it cannot be a security for another debt.
A pledge requires an independent debt.
4
Note the consequence
If such a void pledge is destroyed, the buyer owes nothing because the contract is void.
The destruction goes for nothing.
5
Generalize
This illustrates the broader principle: pledge is only valid for a debt.
The sold item is not a debt but a price guarantee.

الظاهر الغالب من كون قيمة الرهن مساوية للدين أو أكثر من ذلك. قال الفقيه أبو الليث في الفتاوى : رجل دخل المدينة ونزل خاناً فقال صاحب الخان لا ينزل هنا أحد ما لم يعط شيئاً فدفع إليه ثيابه فهلكت عنده؛ إن And as for the sale, because it is guaranteed by other, because it is guaranteed by the price. [Page break] ... Even if the pledge with the sold item is destroyed, it goes for nothing because it is a consideration of falsehood, so nothing is owed to the buyer. He (the author) said: "And it is only valid with a debt, even if promised, and not with other than it." We have explained the meaning, which is that the pledge is collection, and collection is realized in the obligatory, which is the debt. Then the apparent obligation of the debt suffices for the validity of the pledge, and its actual obligation is not required for what we mentioned. He said in al-Hidayah: So if the promised pledge is destroyed, it is destroyed for the amount named of money. He said in Ghayat al-Bayan: There is leniency because it is destroyed for the lesser of its value and what was named from the loan. Do you not see what Imam al-Asbijabi said in Sharh al-Tahawi: If he takes the pledge with the condition that he will lend him such-and-such, and it is destroyed in his hand before he lends him, it is destroyed for the lesser of its value and what was named of the loan. End. Taj al-Shari'ah said in his commentary on the author's statement where he said: "It is destroyed for what was named of money" — in response, this is when the pledge equals the debt in value, and he spoke generally according to custom because the apparent is that the pledge equals the debt. And Saheb al-Inaya followed his lead. I say: There is clear inadequacy, because what is mentioned in the book works when the value of the pledge is equal to or more than that debt; there is no need to specify the case of equality. The truth is that in explanation it should be said: This is when the value of the pledge equals or exceeds the named loan; but if the value is less, it is destroyed for the value of the pledge, as has been determined previously that the pledge is guaranteed by the lesser of its value and the debt. However, the author mentioned his statement "it is destroyed for what was named of the debt" as a general statement following the prevalent custom that the value is equal or more. Al-Faqih Abu al-Layth said in al-Fatawa: A man entered the city and stayed at a caravanserai; the owner said, "No one stays here unless he gives something." So he gave his clothes to him and they were destroyed. If he pledged them for the rent, the pledge is for what is involved; if he took them from him because he thought he was a thief and feared him, the owner guarantees. Thus said 'Isam ibn Yusuf. Al-Faqih Abu al-Layth said: In my view, he does not guarantee because he was not forced to give them. And if he pledges a garment and says, "Hold it for twenty dirhams," and the garment is destroyed with the pledgee before he gives him anything, he owes the value of the garment unless its value exceeds twenty, because the pledge is guaranteed by the lesser of its value and the pledge. He pledges two animals on condition that he lends him one hundred, and the value of one is fifty and the other thirty; he takes possession of the one worth fifty and it is destroyed; he returns fifty because it is guaranteed by value, not by the named amount, like that taken by way of sale. If he wishes to take the other, he may do so, and he is not forced to lend, because the pledge is binding on the side of the pledgor, so what is stipulated on the pledgor in the pledge is binding; but on the side of the pledgee, it is not binding, and the loan is stipulated on the pledgee, so it is not binding on him. And if the other animal dies at the time of the pledge, and they differ over the value of the one that was destroyed with the pledgee, the statement is the pledgee's because the pledgor claims an increase in guarantee and he denies it. And if one of them dies, we look at the value of the remaining one, so the value of the destroyed one appears; then their difference is not considered because it is possible to know the matter of dispute from other than them. Ibn Rustom from...
Source pages p. 450

Commentary
This section continues the discussion of invalid pledges, specifically the sold item (mabi'). The text argues that a sold item is guaranteed by the price (thaman), not by itself, so it cannot be used as a pledge because a pledge must be a debt (dayn) that is obligatory. The author then reiterates the general principle: a pledge is only valid for a debt, even a promised one. The commentary from al-Hidayah, Ghayat al-Bayan, and others clarifies the earlier statement that the promised pledge is destroyed for the named amount. However, later commentators (like the author of al-Bahr al-Raiq himself) refine this: the pledge is actually destroyed for the lesser of its value and the named debt, but out of custom, the text assumed the value is at least equal. The passage then presents a fatwa from Abu al-Layth about a traveler who leaves clothes with a caravanserai owner; the ruling depends on whether it was a pledge or a coerced deposit. Another case: a garment given as a pledge for twenty dirhams is destroyed; the pledgee owes the value up to twenty. Finally, a detailed case of two animals pledged for a loan of one hundred: the pledgee takes one worth fifty, it is destroyed, he owes fifty (the value). He may take the other instead, but is not forced to lend because the condition of lending on the pledgee is not binding. Differences over value are resolved by examining the remaining animal.

Key Terms
المبيع (al-mabi'): The sold item; the object of sale that is already in the buyer's possession.
الرهن لازم في جانب الراهن (al-rahn lazim fi janib al-rahin): The pledge is binding upon the pledgor; he cannot revoke it, but the pledgee is not bound to lend until he does.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Because the seller does not consent.
B) Because the sold item is guaranteed by the price.
C) Because the pledge is not witnessed.
D) Because the debt is not yet due.

Key Takeaway
A sold item cannot be pledged because it is already a security for the price.

Pledge is Only Valid for a Debt: General Rule and Conditions
General principle with proof and subsequent correction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi rule that a pledge is only valid for a debt (even a promised one) and identify the condition that the debt must be legally due or prospective.

Core Idea
A pledge is valid only for a debt; a merely promised debt suffices for validity.

After establishing the invalidity of pledging a sold item, the author states the general rule: a pledge is only valid for a debt (dayn). He then clarifies that even a promised debt (e.g., a future loan) is sufficient because pledge is a means of collection, and collection applies to an obligation. We will unpack the reasoning and the correction that follows about the amount guaranteed.

Guided Path
1
Identify the rule
The author states: 'And it is only valid with a debt, even if promised, and not with other than it.'
This is the foundational principle for pledge validity.
2
Understand the illa
Pledge is a form of collection (istifa'), and collection is realized only for something obligatory (wajib), i.e., a debt.
The debt can be actual or promised.
3
Note the sufficiency of apparent obligation
The apparent obligation of the debt is enough; actual obligation is not required.
This allows pledge for a future loan.
4
Examine the destruction of a promised pledge
According to al-Hidayah, if the promised pledge is destroyed, it is destroyed for the named amount. But later commentators refine this to the lesser of value and named amount.
This correction ensures fairness.
5
Apply the refined rule
The pledge is guaranteed by the lesser of its value and the debt amount.
This is the mufta bihi position.

رهنها من قبل الأجرة فالرهن بما فيه، وإن أخذها منه لأنه ظنه سارقاً فخشي منه يضمن صاحب الخان؛ كذا قال عصام بن يوسف قال الفقيه أبو الليث : وعندي أنه لا يضمن لأنه لم يكن مكرهاً بالدفع إليه . ولو رهن ثوباً فقال أمسكه بعشرين درهماً فهلك الثوب عند المرتهن قبل أن يعطيه شيئاً فعليه قيمة الثوب إلا أن يجاوز قيمته عشرين لأن الرهن مضمون بالأقل من قيمته ومن الرهن رهن دابتين على أن يقرضه مائة وقيمة إحداهما خمسون والأخرى ثلاثون فقبض ما قيمتها خمسون فهلكت يرد خمسين لأن مضمون بالقيمة لا بالمسمى كالمقبوض بجهة البيع فإن بداله أن يأخذ الأخرى له ذلك ولا يجبر على القرض لأن الرهن لازم في جانب الراهن فما شرط على الراهن في الرهن يكون لازماً، وفي حق المرتهن فيه لا يكون لازماً والقرض مشروط على المرتهن فلا يكون لازماً في حقه. ولو نفقت الأخرى عند الرهن واختلف في قيمة التي هلكت عند المرتهن فالقول للمرتهن لأن الراهن يدعي على المرتهن زيادة ضمان وهو ينكر، وإن نفقت إحداهما ينظر إلى قيمة الباقي فتظهر قيمة الهالك فلا يلتفت إلى اختلافهما لأنه أمكن معرفة ما وقع التنازع فيه لا من جهتهما. ابن رستم عن
And as for the sale, because it is guaranteed by other, because it is guaranteed by the price. [Page break] ... Even if the pledge with the sold item is destroyed, it goes for nothing because it is a consideration of falsehood, so nothing is owed to the buyer. He (the author) said: "And it is only valid with a debt, even if promised, and not with other than it." We have explained the meaning, which is that the pledge is collection, and collection is realized in the obligatory, which is the debt. Then the apparent obligation of the debt suffices for the validity of the pledge, and its actual obligation is not required for what we mentioned. He said in al-Hidayah: So if the promised pledge is destroyed, it is destroyed for the amount named of money. He said in Ghayat al-Bayan: There is leniency because it is destroyed for the lesser of its value and what was named from the loan. Do you not see what Imam al-Asbijabi said in Sharh al-Tahawi: If he takes the pledge with the condition that he will lend him such-and-such, and it is destroyed in his hand before he lends him, it is destroyed for the lesser of its value and what was named of the loan. End. Taj al-Shari'ah said in his commentary on the author's statement where he said: "It is destroyed for what was named of money" — in response, this is when the pledge equals the debt in value, and he spoke generally according to custom because the apparent is that the pledge equals the debt. And Saheb al-Inaya followed his lead. I say: There is clear inadequacy, because what is mentioned in the book works when the value of the pledge is equal to or more than that debt; there is no need to specify the case of equality. The truth is that in explanation it should be said: This is when the value of the pledge equals or exceeds the named loan; but if the value is less, it is destroyed for the value of the pledge, as has been determined previously that the pledge is guaranteed by the lesser of its value and the debt. However, the author mentioned his statement "it is destroyed for what was named of the debt" as a general statement following the prevalent custom that the value is equal or more. Al-Faqih Abu al-Layth said in al-Fatawa: A man entered the city and stayed at a caravanserai; the owner said, "No one stays here unless he gives something." So he gave his clothes to him and they were destroyed. If he pledged them for the rent, the pledge is for what is involved; if he took them from him because he thought he was a thief and feared him, the owner guarantees. Thus said 'Isam ibn Yusuf. Al-Faqih Abu al-Layth said: In my view, he does not guarantee because he was not forced to give them. And if he pledges a garment and says, "Hold it for twenty dirhams," and the garment is destroyed with the pledgee before he gives him anything, he owes the value of the garment unless its value exceeds twenty, because the pledge is guaranteed by the lesser of its value and the pledge. He pledges two animals on condition that he lends him one hundred, and the value of one is fifty and the other thirty; he takes possession of the one worth fifty and it is destroyed; he returns fifty because it is guaranteed by value, not by the named amount, like that taken by way of sale. If he wishes to take the other, he may do so, and he is not forced to lend, because the pledge is binding on the side of the pledgor, so what is stipulated on the pledgor in the pledge is binding; but on the side of the pledgee, it is not binding, and the loan is stipulated on the pledgee, so it is not binding on him. And if the other animal dies at the time of the pledge, and they differ over the value of the one that was destroyed with the pledgee, the statement is the pledgee's because the pledgor claims an increase in guarantee and he denies it. And if one of them dies, we look at the value of the remaining one, so the value of the destroyed one appears; then their difference is not considered because it is possible to know the matter of dispute from other than them. Ibn Rustom from...
Source pages p. 450

Commentary
This section continues the discussion of invalid pledges, specifically the sold item (mabi'). The text argues that a sold item is guaranteed by the price (thaman), not by itself, so it cannot be used as a pledge because a pledge must be a debt (dayn) that is obligatory. The author then reiterates the general principle: a pledge is only valid for a debt, even a promised one. The commentary from al-Hidayah, Ghayat al-Bayan, and others clarifies the earlier statement that the promised pledge is destroyed for the named amount. However, later commentators (like the author of al-Bahr al-Raiq himself) refine this: the pledge is actually destroyed for the lesser of its value and the named debt, but out of custom, the text assumed the value is at least equal. The passage then presents a fatwa from Abu al-Layth about a traveler who leaves clothes with a caravanserai owner; the ruling depends on whether it was a pledge or a coerced deposit. Another case: a garment given as a pledge for twenty dirhams is destroyed; the pledgee owes the value up to twenty. Finally, a detailed case of two animals pledged for a loan of one hundred: the pledgee takes one worth fifty, it is destroyed, he owes fifty (the value). He may take the other instead, but is not forced to lend because the condition of lending on the pledgee is not binding. Differences over value are resolved by examining the remaining animal.

Key Terms
الرهن (al-rahn): A pledge; a property given as security for a debt.
استيفاء (istifa'): Collection; the act of taking what is due.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the refined Hanafi ruling, if a promised pledge is destroyed before the debt is created, how much is the pledgee liable for?
A) The full named debt amount.
B) The full value of the pledge.
C) The lesser of the value of the pledge and the named debt.
D) Nothing, because the debt was not yet given.

Key Takeaway
A pledge is valid only for a debt, even a promised one, and if destroyed, the compensation is the lesser of its value and the debt.

Rahn — Pledge
advanced
10 min
Case Studies: Caravanserai Deposit and Pledge of Two Animals
Application of pledge rules to specific cases with differing scholarly opinions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rules of pledge to two practical cases: whether a deposit at a caravanserai is a pledge or a trust, and how to calculate liability when multiple items are pledged for a single loan.

Core Idea
A deposit taken under duress is not a pledge; and when multiple items are pledged, each is guaranteed by its own value up to the debt.

The passage concludes with two practical cases that test the previously established principles. The first case involves a traveler who gives his clothes to a caravanserai owner upon demand. Is it a pledge or a coerced deposit? The second case involves two animals pledged for a single loan, with one destroyed. We will apply the rules of guarantee and the binding nature of the pledge contract.

Guided Path
1
Analyze the caravanserai case
A traveler gives clothes to the owner who demands something before allowing him to stay. Determine whether it is a pledge (for rent) or a coerced deposit.
The ruling depends on the owner's intention: if he saw the traveler as suspicious, it is not a pledge.
2
Apply the rule for the first case
If it is a pledge, the clothes are security for the rent. If it is a coerced deposit, the owner is liable as a guarantor. Abu al-Layth says no liability if not forced.
The difference turns on whether the traveler was compelled (mukrah) or not.
3
Analyze the two-animal case
Two animals (values 50 and 30) are pledged for a loan of 100. The pledgee takes the one worth 50 and it is destroyed. How much is owed?
The pledge is guaranteed by the value of the item taken, not by the total loan.
4
Apply the guarantee rule
The destroyed animal is guaranteed by its value (50) because it is like property taken by sale; it is not for the named loan amount.
The pledgee may take the other animal instead, but is not forced to lend.
5
Handle value disputes
If there is a dispute over the value of the destroyed animal, the pledgee's statement is taken because the pledgor claims a higher guarantee.
If the other animal remains, its value can be used to infer the destroyed one's value.

Source Text

حتى إذا هلك الرهن بالمبيع ذهب بغير شيء لأنه اعتبار الباطل فلا يجب على المشتري شيء . قال رحمه الله : (وإنما يصح بدين ولو موعوداً ولا . بغيره وقد بينا المعنى فيه وهو أن الرهن استيفاء والاستيفاء يتحقق في الواجب وهو الدين، ثم وجوب الدين ظاهراً يكفي لصحة الرهن ولا يشترط وجوبه حقيقة لما ذكرنا . قال في الهداية : فإذا هلك الرهن الموعود هلك بما يسمى من المال قال في غاية البيان : فيه تسامح لأن يهلك بالأقل من In the two-animal case, if the pledgee takes one animal worth 50 and it is destroyed, how much does the pledgee owe?

D) 20, because the animal was only part of the pledge.

Validity of a Pledge for a Debt (Even a Promised One)
Explaining that a pledge is only valid for a debt, and the ruling on a pledge for a promised debt.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a pledge is valid for a promised debt and explain the legal reasoning (istifa' and wujub).

Core Idea
A pledge secures a debt because it is a recovery (istifa'), and recovery only applies to an obligation.

This lesson explores the foundational principle that a pledge (rahn) is only valid for a debt (dayn) according to the Hanafi school. We will see that even a debt that is merely promised (maw'ud) suffices, based on the conceptual link between a pledge and recovery of an obligation.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition of rahn as istifa'
Recognize that a pledge is legally considered a means for the creditor to recover the debt. This recovery (istifa') is the core purpose.
2
Link istifa' to obligation (wajib)
Istifa' is only meaningful when there is an obligation to recover. Without an obligation, there is nothing to recover.
3
Identify the condition for validity
The pledge must secure a debt (dayn), which is an obligation. The Hanafi school holds that even a promised debt has an apparent obligation, sufficient for validity.
4
Conclude the ruling
Thus, a pledge is valid for any debt, existing or promised, but not for other things like guarantees.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يصح حتى إذا هلك ذهب بالثمن فلا يجب على البائع والرهن لا يجوز إلا بالاعيان المضمونة بنفسها ولا يجوز بالأعيان المضمونة بغيرها كالرهن، وإن هلك الرهن بالمبيع ذهب بغير شيء لأنه اعتبار الباطل فلا يجب على المشتري شيء . قال رحمه الله : (وإنما يصح بدين ولو موعوداً ولا . بغيره وقد بينا المعنى فيه وهو أن الرهن استيفاء والاستيفاء يتحقق في الواجب وهو الدين، ثم وجوب الدين ظاهراً يكفي لصحة الرهن ولا يشترط وجوبه حقيقة لما ذكرنا . قال في الهداية : فإذا هلك الرهن الموعود هلك بما يسمى من المال قال في غاية البيان : فيه تسامح لأن يهلك بالأقل من قيمته ومما سمى ) من القرض ألا ترى إلى ما قال الإمام الأسبيجابي في شرح الطحاوي : ولو أخذ الرهن بشرط أن يقرضه كذا فهلك في يده قبل أن يقرضه هلك بالأقل من قيمته ومما سمى له من القرض .اهـ. قال تاج الشريعة في شرح قول المصنف حيث قال هلك بما سمى من المال بمقابلته هذا إذا ساوى الرهن الدين قيمة وإنما أطلق جرياً على العادة إذا الظاهر أن يساوي الرهن الدين ا واقتفى أثره صاحب العناية . أقول : فيه قصور بين فإن ما ذكر في الكتاب كما يتمشى فيما إذا ساوى قيمة الرهن أكثر من ذلك الدين فلا حاجة لتخصيصه بصورة المساواة فالحق أن يقال في البيان هذا إذا ساوى قيمة الرهن ما سمى له من القرض أو كانت قيمته أكثر من ذلك، وأما إذا كانت قيمة الرهن أقل من ذلك فيهلك بقيمة الرهن إذا قد تقدر فيما مر أن الرهن مضمون بالأقل من قيمته ومن الدين ولكن المصنف ذكر هنا قوله حيث يهلك بما سمى له من الدين في صورة الإطلاق جرياً على ما هو الظاهر الغالب من كون قيمة الرهن مساوية للدين أو أكثر من ذلك.
It is valid such that if it is destroyed, it goes with the price, so nothing is obligatory upon the seller. And the pledge is not valid except for assets that are inherently liable, and it is not valid for assets that are liable through something else like a pledge. And if the pledge is destroyed along with the sold item, it goes for nothing because it is a consideration of voidness, so nothing is obligatory upon the buyer. He said: (It is only valid for a debt, even if promised, and not for other things.) And we have explained the meaning, which is that the pledge is a recovery, and recovery is realized in the obligatory, which is debt. Then the apparent obligation of the debt is sufficient for the validity of the pledge, and its actual obligation is not required, as we mentioned. And in Al-Hidayah: So if the promised pledge is destroyed, it is destroyed with whatever amount is named of money. And in Ghayat al-Bayan: There is leniency in this because it is destroyed by the lesser of its value and what was named. From the loan, do you not see what Imam al-Asbijabi said in his commentary on al-Tahawi: If he took the pledge with the condition that he would lend him such-and-such, and it was destroyed in his hand before he lent it, it is destroyed by the lesser of its value and what was named to him of the loan. End. Taj al-Shari'ah said in commentary on the author's statement where he said: it is destroyed by what is named of money, corresponding to it. This is if the pledge equals the debt in value. And he (the author) only made it absolute following the custom, because it appears that the pledge equals the debt. And the author of al-Inayah followed him. I say: There is evident insufficiency in this, because what is mentioned in the book applies when the value of the pledge is equal to or more than that debt, so there is no need to specify it with the case of equality. So the truth is to say in the explanation: This is if the value of the pledge equals what was named of the loan or is more than that. But if the value of the pledge is less than that, it is destroyed by the value of the pledge, as has been established previously that the pledge is liable for the lesser of its value and the debt. But the author mentioned here his statement where it is destroyed by what is named of the debt in absolute terms, following what is apparent and predominant, that the value of the pledge equals or exceeds the debt.
Source pages p. 450

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on the matn of Kanz al-Daqaiq. It begins by affirming that a pledge can be valid for a debt, even if that debt is not yet due (موعود). The reason is that a pledge is a form of 'recovery' (استيفاء) for the creditor, and recovery only makes sense in the context of an obligation (واجب). However, for the validity of the pledge, it is sufficient that the debt appears to be obligatory externally; actual obligation is not required. The commentary then addresses the scenario where the pledge is destroyed: according to Al-Hidayah, it is destroyed by the amount named as the debt. But Ghayat al-Bayan points out that this is a lenient view, and the correct ruling is that the pledge is destroyed by the lesser of its value and the named debt. Imam al-Asbijabi confirms this in his commentary on al-Tahawi. Taj al-Shari'ah qualifies the statement by saying it applies when the pledge's value equals the debt, and the author's absolute statement is based on the common case. The commentator (probably the author of al-Bahr al-Raiq) critiques this and clarifies that the ruling should be: if the pledge's value equals or exceeds the debt, it is destroyed by the named debt; if the pledge's value is less, it is destroyed by its actual value. This is because the pledge is liable for the lesser of its value and the debt, a principle established earlier.

Key Terms
رهن (rahn): A pledge or collateral given to secure a debt.
استيفاء (istifa'): Recovery of a debt; the creditor's act of taking possession of the pledge as a means of recovering the debt.
موعود (maw'ud): A debt that is promised but not yet due or contracted.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is a requirement for a pledge to be valid according to the Hanafi school?
A) The debt must be existing and due.
B) The debt must be a financial obligation, even if promised.
C) The pledge must be in the creditor's possession.
D) The pledge must be of equal value to the debt.

Key Takeaway
A pledge is valid for any debt, including a promised one, because the meaning of rahn as recovery (istifa') only requires an apparent obligation.

Compensation When a Pledge is Destroyed: The Lesser of Value or Debt
Explaining the compensation rule when a pledge for a promised debt is destroyed.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to calculate the compensation when a pledged item is destroyed before the debt is fulfilled, applying the Hanafi rule of the lesser of value or debt.

Core Idea
If a pledge is destroyed, the creditor loses only the lesser of the pledge's value and the debt, preventing unjust enrichment.

This lesson addresses the scenario where a pledged item is destroyed while in the creditor's possession. The Hanafi school has a precise rule to ensure fairness: the creditor compensates by deducting the lesser of the pledge's value and the outstanding debt. We will examine the commentary's refinement of this rule.

Guided Path
1
Understand the scenario
A pledge has been given for a promised loan (or any debt) and is destroyed in the creditor's custody before the debt is fulfilled.
2
Identify the initial ruling
According to Al-Hidayah, the pledge is 'destroyed with the named amount' – meaning the creditor loses the amount of the promised loan.
3
Recognize the refinement
Later commentators clarify that this applies only when the pledge's value equals or exceeds the debt. If the pledge is of lesser value, the creditor loses only the pledge's value.
4
Apply the rule
The compensation is the lesser of the pledge's market value and the debt amount. This prevents the creditor from profiting beyond the debt.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يصح حتى إذا هلك ذهب بالثمن فلا يجب على البائع والرهن لا يجوز إلا بالاعيان المضمونة بنفسها ولا يجوز بالأعيان المضمونة بغيرها كالرهن، وإن هلك الرهن بالمبيع ذهب بغير شيء لأنه اعتبار الباطل فلا يجب على المشتري شيء . قال رحمه الله : (وإنما يصح بدين ولو موعوداً ولا . بغيره وقد بينا المعنى فيه وهو أن الرهن استيفاء والاستيفاء يتحقق في الواجب وهو الدين، ثم وجوب الدين ظاهراً يكفي لصحة الرهن ولا يشترط وجوبه حقيقة لما ذكرنا . قال في الهداية : فإذا هلك الرهن الموعود هلك بما يسمى من المال قال في غاية البيان : فيه تسامح لأن يهلك بالأقل من قيمته ومما سمى ) من القرض ألا ترى إلى ما قال الإمام الأسبيجابي في شرح الطحاوي : ولو أخذ الرهن بشرط أن يقرضه كذا فهلك في يده قبل أن يقرضه هلك بالأقل من قيمته ومما سمى له من القرض .اهـ. قال تاج الشريعة في شرح قول المصنف حيث قال هلك بما سمى من المال بمقابلته هذا إذا ساوى الرهن الدين قيمة وإنما أطلق جرياً على العادة إذا الظاهر أن يساوي الرهن الدين ا واقتفى أثره صاحب العناية . أقول : فيه قصور بين فإن ما ذكر في الكتاب كما يتمشى فيما إذا ساوى قيمة الرهن أكثر من ذلك الدين فلا حاجة لتخصيصه بصورة المساواة فالحق أن يقال في البيان هذا إذا ساوى قيمة الرهن ما سمى له من القرض أو كانت قيمته أكثر من ذلك، وأما إذا كانت قيمة الرهن أقل من ذلك فيهلك بقيمة الرهن إذا قد تقدر فيما مر أن الرهن مضمون بالأقل من قيمته ومن الدين ولكن المصنف ذكر هنا قوله حيث يهلك بما سمى له من الدين في صورة الإطلاق جرياً على ما هو الظاهر الغالب من كون قيمة الرهن مساوية للدين أو أكثر من ذلك.
It is valid such that if it is destroyed, it goes with the price, so nothing is obligatory upon the seller. And the pledge is not valid except for assets that are inherently liable, and it is not valid for assets that are liable through something else like a pledge. And if the pledge is destroyed along with the sold item, it goes for nothing because it is a consideration of voidness, so nothing is obligatory upon the buyer. He said: (It is only valid for a debt, even if promised, and not for other things.) And we have explained the meaning, which is that the pledge is a recovery, and recovery is realized in the obligatory, which is debt. Then the apparent obligation of the debt is sufficient for the validity of the pledge, and its actual obligation is not required, as we mentioned. And in Al-Hidayah: So if the promised pledge is destroyed, it is destroyed with whatever amount is named of money. And in Ghayat al-Bayan: There is leniency in this because it is destroyed by the lesser of its value and what was named. End. Taj al-Shari'ah said in commentary on the author's statement where he said: it is destroyed by what is named of money, corresponding to it. This is if the pledge equals the debt in value. I say: There is evident insufficiency in this, because what is mentioned in the book applies when the value of the pledge is equal to or more than that debt, so there is no need to specify it with the case of equality. Traveler's Pledge to Innkeeper: Liability for Destroyed Goods
Case study from Abu al-Layth's Fatawa on innkeeper liability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on liability when a guest gives clothes to an innkeeper, distinguishing between pledge and wrongful taking, and identify the difference of opinion between 'Isam ibn Yusuf and Abu al-Layth.

Core Idea
When goods are given voluntarily without coercion, the bailee is not liable even if taken under suspicion.

This passage from the Fatawa of Abu al-Layth al-Samarqandi presents a practical case: a traveler at an inn is asked to give something as security for lodging. The ruling distinguishes two intentions – giving as a pledge for rent versus giving because the innkeeper suspects theft – and highlights a subtle difference of opinion on liability if the goods are destroyed. Understanding this case sharpens your ability to apply Hanafi principles of bailment (amanah) and wrongful possession (ghasb).

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage describes two possible reasons the innkeeper received the clothes: (1) as a pledge (rahn) for the rent, or (2) because he suspected the man was a thief and took the clothes out of fear.
The key is the innkeeper's intention at the time of taking possession.
2
Determine the ruling for each scenario
For the first scenario (pledge), the innkeeper is a bailee (amin) and is not liable for destruction unless negligent. For the second scenario (suspicion), 'Isam ibn Yusuf holds the innkeeper liable as a wrongdoer (ghasib).
A pledge creates a trust; taking out of fear creates a trespass.
3
Identify the ikhtilaf
Abu al-Layth disagrees with 'Isam on the second scenario. He argues that if the traveler voluntarily handed over the clothes without coercion, the innkeeper is not liable, even if the innkeeper's motive was suspicion.
This is an intra-Hanafi difference (ikhilaf fi al-madhhab).
4
Analyse the basis of the difference
'Isam focuses on the innkeeper's wrongful intent (suspicion), making him a ghasib. Abu al-Layth focuses on the traveler's voluntary delivery, which makes the innkeeper an amin (trustee) regardless of the innkeeper's motive.
The Hanafi principle: liability follows the nature of possession, not the hidden intention.
5
Apply the ruling to a new case
Consider a hotel requiring a security deposit. If the guest hands over cash voluntarily and it is stolen, is the hotel liable? According to Abu al-Layth's view, if the guest was not forced (e.g., he could have chosen another hotel), the hotel is not liable. According to 'Isam, if the hotel demanded the deposit out of suspicion, it would be liable.
This demonstrates the practical impact of the ikhtilaf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الفقيه أبو الليث في الفتاوى : رجل دخل المدينة ونزل خاناً فقال صاحب الخان لا ينزل هنا أحد ما لم يعط شيئاً فدفع إليه ثيابه فهلكت عنده؛ إن رهنها من قبل الأجرة فالرهن بما فيه، وإن أخذها منه لأنه ظنه سارقاً فخشي منه يضمن صاحب الخان؛ كذا قال عصام بن يوسف قال الفقيه أبو الليث : وعندي أنه لا يضمن لأنه لم يكن مكرهاً بالدفع إليه .
Al-Faqih Abu al-Layth said in al-Fatawa: A man entered the city and stayed at an inn. The innkeeper said: 'No one stays here unless he gives something.' So he gave him his clothes, and they were destroyed. If he pledged them for the rent, then the pledge is for what is in it. But if he took them from him because he thought he was a thief and feared him, then the innkeeper is liable. 'Isam ibn Yusuf said so. Al-Faqih Abu al-Layth said: In my view, he is not liable because he was not forced to give them.
Source pages p. 450

Commentary
This is a ruling from the Fatawa of al-Faqih Abu al-Layth (a Hanafi jurist). It distinguishes two scenarios when a guest gives his clothes to an innkeeper to secure lodging. First, if the clothes are given as a pledge for the rent, then the validity of the pledge depends on the amount owed. The innkeeper is a bailee (amin) and is not liable for destruction unless negligent. Second, if the innkeeper takes the clothes because he suspects the man is a thief and out of fear, then the innkeeper is a wrongdoer (ghasib) and liable. However, Abu al-Layth gives his own opinion that even in the second case, if the man voluntarily gave the clothes without coercion, the innkeeper is not liable. This reflects the Hanafi principle that liability in pledges depends on the nature of possession.

Key Terms
الخان (khan): An inn or caravanserai.
الرهن (rahn): Pledge or collateral given as security for a debt.
الضمان (daman): Liability, guarantee, or compensation for loss.
الإكراه (ikrah): Coercion or duress that negates free consent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu al-Layth's own view, what is the basis for the innkeeper not being liable in the case where he took the clothes out of suspicion?
A) The innkeeper had good intentions.
B) The traveler was not forced to give the clothes.
C) The clothes were given as a pledge.
D) The innkeeper feared for his safety.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, voluntary delivery of goods without coercion establishes a trust (amanah), and the bailee is not liable for accidental loss, regardless of the bailee's motives.

Pledge Without a Loan: The Lesser of Two Values
Ruling on a pledge where the creditor takes possession but does not give the loan.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a pledge where the loan is not given and apply the rule that the pledgee's compensation is the lesser of the pledged item's value and the promised debt.

Core Idea
A pledge without a loan is void; the creditor's liability is the lesser of the item's value and the intended debt.

This passage addresses a scenario where a pledge is given but the loan is never disbursed. Even though the pledge is invalid due to the absence of a debt, the creditor's possession of the pledged item imposes liability. The Hanafi school applies a unique rule to determine compensation: it is the lesser of the item's actual value and the amount of the loan promised.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine the ruling on the pledge transaction itself. The pledge is invalid (void/fasid) because the loan was not given. The promise to hold for a debt does not create a valid pledge without actual disbursement.
The absence of a debt is a condition for validity; without it, the pledge is not binding.
2
Determine the status of the pledgee's possession
Since the pledge is invalid, the pledgee's possession is wrongful (like ghasb). He is therefore liable for the item's destruction during his possession.
Possession without right triggers liability in Hanafi fiqh.
3
Apply the liability rule
The Hanafi rule is that the pledgor's liability for the pledge is the lesser of its value and the debt. Here, the debt never materialized, but the promised amount (20 dirhams) serves as a cap on liability. The creditor must pay the lesser of the garment's actual value and 20 dirhams.
The phrase 'إلا أن يجاوز قيمته عشرين' indicates the exception when value exceeds the stated amount.
4
State the final compensation
The pledgee must pay the value of the garment if it is 20 dirhams or less. If the value exceeds 20, he pays only 20 dirhams. This ensures fairness: the creditor's liability is limited to the amount he promised to lend.
No compensation for lost expectation; only actual loss capped by the intended loan.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو رهن ثوباً فقال أمسكه بعشرين درهماً فهلك الثوب عند المرتهن قبل أن يعطيه شيئاً فعليه قيمة الثوب إلا أن يجاوز قيمته عشرين لأن الرهن مضمون بالأقل من قيمته ومن الرهن
And if a man pledged a garment and said: 'Hold it for twenty dirhams,' and the garment was destroyed with the pledgee before he gave him anything, he is liable for the value of the garment unless its value exceeds twenty, because the pledge is liable for the lesser of its value and the pledge [debt].
Source pages p. 450

Commentary
This is a clear ruling: if a person gives a garment as a pledge to secure a loan of twenty dirhams, and the creditor takes the garment but never gives the loan, the transaction is defective. The pledge is not valid because there is no debt. The creditor is a wrongful possessor and must compensate the debtor. However, the compensation is limited to the lesser of the garment's value and the amount promised (twenty dirhams). This aligns with the Hanafi principle that a pledge is liable for the lesser of its value and the debt it secures.

Key Terms
رهن (rahn): Pledge; a contract where property is held as security for a debt.
مرتهن (murtahin): The pledgee (creditor) who holds the pledged item.
عشرين درهماً (ishrina dirhaman): The amount of the intended loan (20 dirhams).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a man gives his garment as a pledge for a loan of 20 dirhams, and the creditor takes it but does not give the loan, and the garment is destroyed, what does the creditor owe?
A) The full value of the garment.
B) 20 dirhams.
C) The lesser of the garment's value and 20 dirhams.
D) Nothing, because the pledge was void.

Key Takeaway
In a pledge without a loan, the creditor's liability is the lesser of the pledged item's value and the promised loan amount.

Pledging Two Animals for a Loan
Case of pledging two animals of different values for a loan of 100, with one destroyed.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when one of two pledged animals is destroyed and determine the pledgee's liability.

Core Idea
Pledged property is liable for its value, not the debt amount.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq illustrates a scenario where two animals of different values are pledged for a loan. The ruling clarifies that the pledgee's liability is based on the actual value of the destroyed item, and he has the option to take the remaining animal but cannot be forced to lend.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the core question
The scenario: two animals valued 50 and 30 are pledged for a loan of 100. The pledgee takes possession of the animal worth 50, which is then destroyed. The key question is how much the pledgee must return.
2
Understand the principle: liability for value
The passage states that the pledge is liable for its value, not the named amount. This is compared to property taken by sale – liability is based on market value.
This principle is rooted in the idea that the pledge is a security, and its value at the time of taking possession determines the liability.
3
Apply the principle to the case
Since the destroyed animal was worth 50, the pledgee must return 50 dirhams, not the full loan of 100.
4
Consider the pledgee's options and loan obligation
The pledgee may choose to take the other animal (worth 30) instead of returning 50, but he cannot be forced to give the loan. The pledge is binding on the pledgor only.
The loan itself is not binding on the pledgee because it was a condition stipulated on him.
5
Handle dispute resolution if both animals die
If the other animal also dies and there is a dispute over the value of the destroyed one, the pledgee's word is taken because the pledgor claims a higher liability. If only one dies, the value of the remaining one is used as evidence.
This prevents reliance on conflicting statements when an objective measure exists.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو رهن دابتين على أن يقرضه مائة وقيمة إحداهما خمسون والأخرى ثلاثون فقبض ما قيمتها خمسون فهلكت يرد خمسين لأن مضمون بالقيمة لا بالمسمى كالمقبوض بجهة البيع فإن بداله أن يأخذ الأخرى له ذلك ولا يجبر على القرض لأن الرهن لازم في جانب الراهن فما شرط على الراهن في الرهن يكون لازماً، وفي حق المرتهن فيه لا يكون لازماً والقرض مشروط على المرتهن فلا يكون لازماً في حقه. ولو نفقت الأخرى عند الرهن واختلف في قيمة التي هلكت عند المرتهن فالقول للمرتهن لأن الراهن يدعي على المرتهن زيادة ضمان وهو ينكر، وإن نفقت إحداهما ينظر إلى قيمة الباقي فتظهر قيمة الهالك فلا يلتفت إلى اختلافهما لأنه أمكن معرفة ما وقع التنازع فيه لا من جهتهما.
And if he pledged two riding animals on the condition that he would lend him one hundred, and the value of one is fifty and the other thirty, and he took the one worth fifty and it was destroyed, he must return fifty, because it is liable for its value, not the named amount, like property taken by way of sale. And if he wishes to take the other, he may, and he is not compelled to give the loan, because the pledge is binding upon the pledgor, so what is stipulated upon the pledgor in the pledge is binding, but regarding the pledgee it is not binding, and the loan is stipulated upon the pledgee, so it is not binding upon him. And if the other one died while in pledge, and they differed over the value of the one that was destroyed with the pledgee, the word is the pledgee's because the pledgor claims an increase in liability which the pledgee denies. And if one of them died, the value of the remaining one is looked at to determine the value of the destroyed one, so their disagreement is not considered because it became possible to know what was disputed not from their statements.
Source pages p. 450

Commentary
This passage presents a detailed case: Two animals are pledged as collateral for a loan of 100 dirhams. The animals have different values. The creditor takes possession of the more valuable one (worth 50) and it is destroyed. The ruling: the creditor must return 50 dirhams, the value of that animal, because the pledge is liable for its value, not the named loan amount. The creditor has the option to take the other animal instead, but he cannot be forced to give the loan; the loan is not binding on him. If the other animal also dies, and there is a dispute about the value of the destroyed animal, the pledgee's word is accepted because the pledgor claims a higher liability. If one animal dies, the remaining one's value serves as evidence for the destroyed one's value, so no reliance on their statements.

Key Terms
دابتين (dabbatayn): Two riding animals (e.g., horses or camels).
قيمة (qīmah): Market value of the pledged item.
قبض (qabd): Taking possession of the pledge.
ضمان (ḍamān): Liability or guarantee.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the case of two animals pledged for a loan of 100, if the creditor takes and destroys the animal worth 50, what must he return?
A) 100 dirhams.
B) 50 dirhams.
C) The value of the other animal.
D) Nothing, because the pledge is void.

Key Takeaway
The pledgee's liability is limited to the value of the pledge item that was destroyed, not the amount of the loan.

Conditional Pledge that Becomes a Sale
Ruling on a pledge with a condition that it becomes a sale.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on a conditional pledge that becomes a sale and identify the proof from hadith that prohibits suspending a pledge.

Core Idea
A pledge cannot be made conditional on a future event; it must be immediately effective.

In this brief passage from Al-Bahr al-Raiq, we learn about a specific type of conditional pledge where the creditor stipulates that if the debt is not paid by a certain date, the pledged item becomes a sale in satisfaction of the debt. Imam Muhammad declares this arrangement impermissible, citing the prophetic hadith 'Do not suspend the pledge.' This lesson unpacks the ruling, the evidence, and why such conditions are void in the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the transaction and condition
Read the passage carefully: a man pledges a garment and says, 'If I do not pay you by such-and-such date, then it is a sale for what you owe me.' This is a conditional pledge with a future contingency.
The condition makes the pledge's outcome dependent on a future event.
2
State the ruling
Imam Muhammad says, 'It is not permissible.' This means the condition is invalid and the entire arrangement is disallowed.
In Hanafi fiqh, 'la yajuz' typically means the act is void (batil) or prohibited.
3
Identify the dalil
The passage cites the hadith 'لا يعلق الرهن' (Do not suspend the pledge). This is the proof for the ruling.
This hadith is narrated in the Sunan of Abu Dawood and Tirmidhi, among others.
4
Understand the prohibition
The hadith prohibits 'suspending' (ta'liq) the pledge. A pledge must be immediately effective; it cannot be made contingent on a future event like non-payment.
This preserves the nature of rahn as a current security, not a conditional future transaction.
5
Apply to similar cases
Any condition that makes the fulfillment of the pledge dependent on a future uncertain event is invalid. The pledge itself may remain valid if the condition is removed.
For example, if the condition is struck out, the pledge stands as a regular security.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ابن رستم عن محمد رحمهما الله تعالى : رجل رهن رجلاً ثوباً فقال له إن لم أعطك إلى كذا وكذا فهو بيع بمالك علي قال : لا يجوز وقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام لا يعلق الرهن» هو هذا.
Ibn Rustam from Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on them): A man pledged a garment to a man and said, 'If I do not give you by such-and-such, then it is a sale for what you owe me.' He said: It is not permissible. And the saying of the Prophet (peace be upon him), 'Do not suspend the pledge,' refers to this.
Source pages p. 450

Commentary
This narration from Ibn Rustam narrating from Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani prohibits a conditional pledge where the pledge automatically becomes a sale if the debt is not paid by a deadline. Such a condition is invalid because it 'suspends' the pledge, making its legal effect contingent on a future event, which is against the nature of a pledge contract. The hadith of the Prophet (SAW) 'لا يعلق الرهن' (do not suspend the pledge) is cited as the proof. In the Hanafi school, a pledge must be immediately effective; conditions that suspend or postpone the rights are void.

Key Terms
لا يعلق الرهن (la yu'allaqu al-rahn): A hadith prohibiting conditional or suspended pledges.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling if a person pledges an item with the condition that it becomes a sale if the debt is not paid by a certain date?
A) It is valid because it secures the debt.
B) It is invalid because it suspends the pledge.
C) It is valid if the item is worth the debt.
D) It is valid only if the creditor agrees.

Key Takeaway
A pledge must be unconditional and immediately effective; any condition that makes its outcome contingent on a future event is invalid per the hadith 'la yu'allaqu al-rahn'.

Usurper Pledging Usurped Property: Liability and Validity
Ruling with reasoning on usurper's pledge

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when a usurper pledges the usurped property to the owner and explain the basis for the owner's liability.

Core Idea
A usurper's pledge of usurped property is invalid, but the owner's liability is limited to the lesser of two values.

This lesson examines a unique scenario: a usurper (ghasib) who wrongfully takes property then pledges that same property to its rightful owner. The Hanafi jurists rule on the resulting liability and the validity of the pledge. Understanding this ruling clarifies how liability (daman) operates even when a contract is defective.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and the act
The usurper (ghasib) is the one who wrongfully possessed the property. He then pledges that property to the person from whom it was taken (the owner). The act is the pledge (rahn).
2
Understand the liability rule
The owner is liable for the lesser of the value of the usurped property and the value of the pledge. This is compensation for taking possession as a pledge.
The owner takes the property 'on the basis of liability' (daman).
3
Analyze the reason for liability
The owner receives the property as a pledge, but since there is no debt (the usurped property is not a debt), the pledge is not valid. However, the owner's possession triggers liability to return the lesser value.
The key principle: a valid pledge requires a debt, which is absent here.
4
Grasp the invalidity of the pledge
The text states the pledge is 'not valid' (ghayr sahih). In Hanafi terms, it is fasid (defective) because the essential condition of a debt is missing.
Despite invalidity, the possession is still treated as a pledge for liability purposes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو رهن الغاصب بالمغصوب رهناً والمغصوب منه ضمن الأقل من قيمة المغصوب وقيمة الرهن لأنه أخذه على جهة الضمان وليس يكون المغصوب ديناً يدفع به رهناً ولكنه لما رهنه صار رهناً وإن لم يكن صحيحاً.
And if the usurper pledged the usurped property as a pledge, and the one from whom it was usurped, he is liable for the lesser of the value of the usurped property and the value of the pledge, because he took it on the basis of liability. And the usurped property is not a debt for which a pledge can be given, but when he pledged it, it became a pledge though not valid.
Source pages p. 451

Commentary
This case deals with a usurper (ghasib) who takes someone else's property without permission and then pledges that very property to the rightful owner. The ruling: the usurper is liable to the owner for the lesser of the value of the usurped property and the value of the pledge. This is because the usurper held the property wrongfully. The pledge itself is not valid since there is no debt between them, but the possession is treated as a liability. The Hanafi school emphasizes that a pledge requires a valid debt; here there is none, so the transaction is substantively invalid, but the owner's right to compensation remains.

Key Terms
الغاصب (al-ghasib): The one who wrongfully takes and possesses someone else's property.
الرهن (ar-rahn): A pledge; a contract where property is given as security for a debt.
الضمان (ad-daman): Liability; obligation to compensate for loss or damage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a usurper pledges the usurped property to the owner, what is the usurper's liability?
A) He is not liable because the owner accepted.
B) He is liable for the full value of the usurped property.
C) He is liable for the lesser of the value of the usurped property and the value of the pledge.
D) He is liable for the full value of the pledge.

Key Takeaway
A usurper's pledge of usurped property to the owner is not a valid pledge, and the owner's liability is limited to the lesser of the property's value and the pledge's value.

The Validity of a Pledge for Salam Capital and Exchange Price: The Hanafi Position vs. Zufar
Intra-Hanafi dispute on pledge validity for salam and sarf debts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on pledging for salam capital and exchange price, explain the proof, and differentiate it from Zufar's objection.

Core Idea
A pledge is security, not recovery; thus valid even where direct exchange is prohibited.

In this passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq, the author affirms a key principle: a pledge (rahn) is permissible even for debts arising from salam and currency exchange (sarf). This is despite the strict prohibition of riba and exchange rules in those contracts. The Hanafi school argues that the pledge is merely a security (istithaq), not an actual exchange. Here we examine the proof and the objection raised by Zufar, a fellow Hanafi scholar whose view was not adopted.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue and the transactions
The passage discusses pledging for three items: the capital of salam (ra's mal al-salam), the price of currency exchange (thaman al-sarf), and the object of salam (al-muslim fihi). All are debts.
Salam is a forward sale; sarf is exchange of gold/silver.
2
State the Hanafi ruling
The Hanafi position is that a pledge is valid for all three, as stated by the author: 'يجوز الرهن بهذه الأشياء'. This is the mufta bihi view.
3
Understand Zufar's objection
Zufar argues that the purpose of a pledge is recovery (istifa'), which involves substitution (istibdal), and substitution is forbidden in these transactions because it resembles riba.
Zufar's view is known but not acted upon.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafi rebuttal distinguishes between recovery in substance and security. The pledge is only a security; recovery happens through monetary value, not the specific object. The pledged item remains a trust (amana) with the pledgee.
Evidence: expenses like maintenance and shroud are on the pledgor, confirming the object is not owned by the pledgee.
5
Determine the practical consequence
Under the Hanafi school, such pledges are valid; under Zufar, they would be invalid. The mufta bihi follows the majority Hanafi view allowing them.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : ( وبرأس مال السلم وثمن الصرف والمسلم فيه) أي يجوز الرهن بهذه الأشياء. وقال زفر : لا يجوز لأن حكمه الاستيفاء وذلك بالاستبدال والاستبدال حرام في بدل الصرف والسلم. ولنا لأنه استيثاق من الوجه الذي بينا وهو المقصود بالرهن وإنما يصير مستوفياً بالمالية لا بالعين ولهذا تكون عينه أمانة في يده حتى تجب نفقته حياً وكفنه ميتاً على الراهن، ولو كان مستوفياً به لوجب على الراهن وهما من حيث المالية جنس واحد فيجوز استيفاء لا مبادلة .
He said: (And for the capital of salam, the price of currency exchange, and the object of salam.) i.e., it is permissible to pledge for these things. And Zufar said: It is not permissible because its ruling is recovery, and that involves exchange, and exchange is forbidden in the case of price of exchange and salam. And our proof: It is security from the aspect we have stated, and that is the purpose of the pledge. It becomes recovery by virtue of monetary value, not by the actual object itself. For this reason, its actual object is a trust in his hand, so its maintenance while alive and its shroud when dead are the responsibility of the pledgor. And if it were recovery [in actual sense], it would be the responsibility of the pledgee. And they are of the same genus in terms of monetary value, so recovery is allowed, not exchange.
Source pages p. 451

Commentary
This is a clear inter-madhhab dispute. The matn of Kanz al-Daqaiq states that a pledge is valid for the capital of a salam (advance payment for future delivery) contract, for the price in a currency exchange (sarf) transaction, and for the object of salam. These are all debts. Zufar, a later Hanafi scholar, disagrees, arguing that taking a pledge for such debts involves 'recovery' (istifa') through exchange, which is prohibited in sarf and salam because it resembles riba or gharar. The Hanafi response is that the pledge is merely a security (istithaq), not actual recovery. The creditor holds the pledged item as a trust (amana), not as ownership. The recovery is only via the monetary value, so it does not constitute an exchange. Thus, it is permissible. The commentary emphasizes that the pledge's purpose is security, not the exchange itself.

Key Terms
رأس مال السلم (ra's mal al-salam): The capital paid in advance in a salam contract.
ثمن الصرف (thaman al-sarf): The price in a currency exchange contract.
المسلم فيه (al-muslim fihi): The object of salam (the goods to be delivered).
استيثاق (istithaq): Security; the purpose of a pledge is to secure a debt.
استيفاء (istifa'): Recovery; taking possession of something in lieu of a debt.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why is a pledge valid for the capital of a salam contract?
A) Because the pledge itself is a form of exchange that completes the contract.
B) Because the pledge is merely a security, not an exchange, and the pledged item remains a trust.
C) Because the capital of salam is not considered a debt, so a pledge is allowed.
D) Because Zufar's objection was based on a weak hadith.

Key Takeaway
A pledge for salam capital or exchange price is valid because it functions as a security, not as an exchange, and the creditor holds the pledged item as a trust.

Continuation of Pledge After Rescission: Qiyas vs. Istihsan
Continuation of rulings on pledge after rescission.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle of istihsan to determine the status of a pledge when the underlying debt is substituted through rescission or settlement.

Core Idea
Through istihsan, a pledge continues to secure the substituted debt even when qiyas would release it.

In Hanafi muamalat, the security interest of a pledge survives changes in the debt's form. This lesson explores a key scenario: after a salam contract is rescinded and the parties settle for the capital (instead of the goods), does the pledge remain valid? The passage contrasts the strict analogy (qiyas) with juristic preference (istihsan), showing how Hanafi jurists prioritise practical justice over rigid analogy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core scenario
A buyer pays salam (advanced payment) for food, and the seller pledges a slave worth the food. Later they settle for the capital repayment. Determine the status of the pledge.
The debt changes from the salam goods to the capital.
2
Apply qiyas (analogy)
Qiyas would say the pledge is released because the capital is a new debt, not a substitute. The original debt (food) is gone, so the pledge fails.
Qiyas focuses on the literal change in obligation.
3
Apply istihsan (juristic preference)
Istihsan treats the capital as a substitute for the salam object; the pledge continues because it was originally taken for the salam obligation, and the capital now stands in its place.
Istihsan preserves the security interest.
4
Work through the subsidiary examples
The passage gives further cases: when the slave is destroyed, when the debt is replaced by barley instead of wheat, etc. Each confirms that the pledge follows the substitute debt.
These examples illustrate the consistent application of istihsan.
5
State the Hanafi mufta bihi
The relied-upon ruling is that the pledge remains valid for the substituted debt. This is based on istihsan as a primary source.
Authority: al-Muhit (al-Sarakhsi) and al-Hidayah (al-Marghinani).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال في المحيط : ولو اشترى عبداً ثم تقابضا ثم تفاسخا كان للمشتري أن يحبس المبيع حتى يستوفي الثمن لأن الفسخ نزل منزلة البيع، وكذلك لو سلم المبيع وأخذ بالرهن رهناً ثم تقايلا كان له أن يحبس الرهن حتى يقبض المبيع، فإن هلك الرهن في يده هلك بالثمن على ما بينا أسلم خمسمائة في طعام فرهن منه عبداً يساوي الطعام وقبضه ثم صالح على رأس المال فالقياس أن لا يقبض الراهن العبد ورأس المال ديـن عـليه. وفي الاستحسان يجعل رهناً بدينه ولا يكون مضموناً. وجه القياس أن رأس المال غير المسلم فيه حقيقة وحكماً لأنه ليس ببدل عن الطعام لأن الطعام وجب بالعقد، ورأس المال بالإقالة وهما ضدان فما وجب بأحدهما لا يعتبر بدلاً عن الآخر فالرهن بالطعام لا يكون رهناً به . ا به. وجه الاستحسان أن رأس المال بدل عن المسلم فيه قائم مقامه لأنه كان بدلاً له في العقد وبالإقالة والصلح لما أسقط حقه في المسلم فيه عاد حقه إلى بدله لأنه وإن كان ديناً حادثاً لكن لما قام مقام المسلم فيه عاد حقه إلى بدله لأنه وإن كان إثباتاً وإسقاطاً فالرهن بالمسلم فيه يكون رهناً بما قام مقامه كالرهن بالمغصوب رهن بقيمته لأنها قائمة مقامه، فإن استوفى رأس المال ثم هلك عنده العبد من غير صنع يعطيه المرتهن مثل الطعام الذي كان على المسلم إليه ويأخذ منه رأس ماله أقرض رجلاً كر حنطة وارتهن منه ثوباً قيمة الكر وصالحه من عليه الحنطة على كر شعير بعينه يصير الثوب رهناً بالشعير، فإذا هلك يهلك مضموناً بالحنطة لأنه الحنطة فصار كما لو برئ بالإيفاء، ويجوز أن يكون الرهن رهناً ولا بريء عن يكون مضموناً كزوائد الرهن يكون محبوساً ولا يكون مضموناً، وذلك لأن الرهن استيفاء حكمي والاستيفاء حكمي لا يربو على الاستيفاء الحقيقي. ولو استوفى المسلم فيه حقيقة ثم تقايلا السلم صحت الإقالة ويرد عليه طعاماً ويأخذ رأس ماله فكذا إذا اصطلحا بعد
It is said in al-Muhit: And if a person bought a slave, then they took possession and then rescinded, the buyer has the right to detain the sold item until he receives the price, because the rescission is treated as a sale. Similarly, if he delivered the sold item and took a pledge, then they rescinded, he has the right to detain the pledge until he receives the sold item. And if the pledge is destroyed in his hand, it is destroyed by the price, as we have explained. Another case: He paid 500 as salam for food, and he pledged a slave worth the food and took possession. Then he settled for the capital. The analogy is that the pledgor does not take back the slave, and the capital is a debt against him. In istihsan, it is made a pledge for his debt and is not liable. The basis of analogy is that the capital is not the salam object in reality and in ruling, because it is not a substitute for the food; the food became due by the contract and the capital by rescission, they are opposites. The basis of istihsan is that the capital is a substitute for the salam object, taking its place, because it was a substitute in the contract, and by rescission he gives up his right in the salam object, so his right returns to its substitute. And if he then receives the capital and then the slave is destroyed without his action, the pledgee must give the food that was owed to the debtor and take his capital. Another case: He lent a man a load of wheat and took a garment as pledge worth the load, and then he settled with him for a load of barley specifically, the garment becomes a pledge for the barley. If it is destroyed, it is destroyed liable for the wheat, because it was the original debt. This is as if he became free by payment. And it is permissible for the pledge to be a pledge and be liable, just as the increase of the pledge is detained but not liable. This is because the pledge is a legal recovery, and legal recovery does not exceed actual recovery. And if he actually received the salam object and then they rescinded, the rescission is valid and he returns the food and takes his capital. Similarly, if they settle afterwards...
Source pages p. 451

Commentary
This passage from al-Muhit (likely by al-Sarakhsi?) discusses various scenarios involving pledges and salam contracts. The first scenario: if a buyer detains the sold item after rescission, he can continue to detain it for the price. Similarly, if a pledge was taken, after rescission the pledge can be held for the substitute debt. The key principle is that the pledge secures the debt, and when the debt changes form (e.g., from salam object to capital), the pledge follows the new debt through istihsan. The analogy (qiyas) would say the pledge is released, but istihsan allows it to continue. This is a typical Hanafi approach of preferring istihsan over qiyas in muamalat. The passage also covers cases where the debt is replaced by a different commodity, such as wheat substituted by barley. The ruling is that the pledge secures the original debt unless the parties agree on a specific substitute. The detailed examples illustrate how Hanafi jurists apply the principles of istihsan to preserve the security interest of the pledge.

Key Terms
الاستحسان (al-istihsan): Juristic preference overriding analogy to avoid hardship.
القياس (al-qiyas): Analogical reasoning.
البدل (al-badal): Substitute. In this context, the capital is a badal for the salam object.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the passage, why does istihsan override qiyas in the case of a pledge after salam rescission?
A) Because qiyas is not a valid source in Hanafi fiqh.
B) Because the capital is considered a substitute for the salam object.
C) Because the pledgee agrees to release the pledge.
D) Because the contract of salam is invalid.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school, through istihsan, allows a pledge to continue securing a debt after rescission if the new debt is a substitute for the original, preserving the creditor's security.
